<div class=\"overlay-text\">\n            <p>The American Astronomical Society (AAS), established in 1899 and based in Washington, DC, is the major organization of professional astronomers in North America. Its membership of about 7,000 individuals also includes physicists, mathematicians, geologists, engineers, and others whose research and educational interests lie within the broad spectrum of subjects comprising contemporary astronomy. The mission of the AAS is to enhance and share humanity's scientific understanding of the universe.</p> <p><a href=\"https://aas.org/\">https://aas.org/</a></div>\n        <p class=\"ta-c mt-1 mb-2\"><a href=\"\"></a></p>\n    </div>\n</span>\n<!-- Partner logo tile ends -->\n\n<!-- Partner logo tile starts -->\n<a href=\"\" class=\"overlay-launch\">\n    <img border=\"0\" src=\"https://cms.iopscience.org/7ebd1b32-0439-11e9-b401-cfe9679c40e3/iop-2016.png?guest=true\" alt=\"The Institute of Physics, find out more\">\n</a>\n<span class=\"overlay-set\">\n    <div class=\"tint-screen\"></div>\n    <div class=\"overlay-panel\">\n        <a title=\"Close\"  class=\"close-icon close-overlay\" tabindex=\"0\" role=\"button\" aria-describedby=\"close-icon-description\"><span class=\"icon-close\"></span></a>\n        <p id=\"close-icon-description\" class=\"offscreen-hidden\">Click here to close this overlay, or press the \"Escape\" key on your keyboard.</p>\n        <div class=\"overlay-img\">\n            <a href=\"\">\n                <img border=\"0\" src=\"https://cms.iopscience.org/7ebd1b32-0439-11e9-b401-cfe9679c40e3/iop-2016.png?guest=true\" alt=\"The Institute of Physics, find out more\">\n            </a>\n        </div>\n        <div class=\"overlay-text\">\n            <p>The Institute of Physics (IOP) is a leading scientific society promoting physics and bringing physicists together for the benefit of all. It has a worldwide membership of around 50 000 comprising physicists from all sectors, as well as those with an interest in physics. It works to advance physics research, application and education; and engages with policy makers and the public to develop awareness and understanding of physics. Its publishing company, IOP Publishing, is a world leader in professional scientific communications.</p> <p><a href=\"https://www.iop.org\">https://www.iop.org</a></p></div>\n        <p class=\"ta-c mt-1 mb-2\"><a href=\"\"></a></p>\n    </div>\n</span>\n<!-- Partner logo tile ends -->\n\n<p></p>\n                </div>\n        </div>\n    </div>\n<!-- Branded journal header ends -->\n</div> <!-- end secondary-header -->\n<!-- Secondary header ends --><div class=\"da1-da2\" itemscope=\"\" itemtype=\"http://schema.org/ScholarlyArticle\" id=\"page-content\">\n            <div class=\"da1 ta1 article-head\">\n                <!-- Start Eyebrow block, containing Surtitle and Labels -->\n<div class=\"eyebrow\">\n\t<!-- Start Collection Labels -->\n    <!-- End Collection Labels --> </div>\n<!-- End Eyebrow block -->\n\n<h1 itemprop=\"headline\" class=\"wd-jnl-art-title\">VLT/<I>MAGELLAN</I> SPECTROSCOPY OF 29 STRONG LENSING SELECTED GALAXY CLUSTERS</h1>\n\n<p class=\"mb-0\">\n\t<span data-authors=\"\">\n\t\t<span itemtype=\"http://schema.org/Person\" itemprop=\"author\" class=\"nowrap\"><span itemprop=\"name\">Mauricio Carrasco</span><sup>1,2</sup></span>, <span itemtype=\"http://schema.org/Person\" itemprop=\"author\" class=\"nowrap\"><span itemprop=\"name\">L. <span class=\"icon-newtab\">\n            </a></p>\n        </div>\n\n    <!-- End Email Alert -->\n<!-- End Email Alert --><!-- Start Citation Alert Link -->\n                        <p>\n<div class=\"jnl-notifications-wrapper\">\n    <a class=\"wd-jnl-email-alert\" href=\"https://myiopscience.iop.org/signin?origin=a0&return=https%3A%2F%2Fiopscience.iop.org%2Fmyiopscience%2Falerts%2Fsubscribe%3FarticleID%3D0004-637X%2F834%2F2%2F210\"><span\n            class=\"icon-alarm icon-alarm--jhp\"></span>Create citation alert</a>\n</div><!-- End Citation Alert Link -->\n                    </div>\n\t\t</div>\n    </div>\n    <!-- Article information ends -->\n<!-- Start Info top-->\n    <!-- end Info top --><div class=\"article-content\">\n\t<span id=\"articleId\" class=\"hide\">0004-637X/834/2/210</span>\n<!-- Start Abstract -->\n    <h2 id=\"artAbst\" class=\"collapse-blocked\">Abstract</h2>\n        <!-- Start article video abstract -->\n\t<!-- End article video abstract -->\n\t<div class=\"article-text wd-jnl-art-abstract cf\" itemprop=\"description\">\n            <P>We present an extensive spectroscopic follow-up campaign of 29 strong lensing (SL) selected galaxy clusters discovered primarily in the Second Red-Sequence Cluster Survey (RCS-2). Our spectroscopic analysis yields redshifts for 52 gravitational arcs present in the core of our galaxy clusters, which correspond to 35 distinct background sources that are clearly distorted by the gravitational potential of these clusters. These lensed galaxies span a wide redshift range of 0.8\u00a0\u2264\u00a0<I>z</I>\u00a0\u2264\u00a02.9, with a median redshift of <I>z</I><SUB><I>s</I></SUB>\u00a0=\u00a01.8\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.1. We also measure reliable redshifts for 1004 cluster members, allowing us to obtain robust velocity dispersion measurements for 23 of these clusters, which we then use to determine their dynamical masses by using a simulation-based <I>\u03c3</I><SUB>DM</SUB>\u00a0\u2212\u00a0<I>M</I><SUB>200</SUB> scaling relation. The redshift and mass ranges covered by our SL sample are 0.22\u00a0\u2264\u00a0<I>z</I>\u00a0\u2264\u00a01.01 and <IMG ALIGN=\"MIDDLE\" alt=\"$5\\times {10}^{13}\\leqslant {M}_{200}/{h}_{70}^{-1}\\,{M}_{\\odot }\\leqslant 1.9\\times {10}^{15}$\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/834/2/210/apjaa3c3eieqn1.gif\"></IMG>, respectively. We analyze and quantify some possible effects that might bias our mass estimates, such as the presence of substructure, the region where cluster members are selected for spectroscopic follow-up, the final number of confirmed members, and line-of-sight effects. We find that 10 clusters of our sample with <I>N</I><SUB>mem</SUB> <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/gsim.gif\" alt=\"gsim\" align=\"baseline\" /> 20 show signs of dynamical substructure. However, the velocity data of only one system is inconsistent with a uni-modal distribution. We therefore assume that the substructures are only marginal and not of comparable size to the clusters themselves. Consequently, our velocity dispersion and mass estimates can be used as <I>priors</I> for SL mass reconstruction studies and also represent an important step toward a better understanding of the properties of the SL galaxy cluster population.</P></div>\n    <!-- End abstract -->\n<!-- Start Export citation and abstract tools -->\n\t<p>\n\t\t<small>Export citation and abstract</small>\n\t\t<span class=\"btn-multi-block\">\n\t\t\t<a title=\"Export BibTex\">BibTeX</a>\n\t\t\t<a href=\"/export?articleId=0004-637X/834/2/210&doi=10.3847/1538-4357/834/2/210&exportFormat=iopexport_ris&exportType=abs&navsubmit=Export+abstract\" class=\"btn btn-primary wd-btn-cit-abs-ris\" title=\"Export RIS\">RIS</a>\n\t\t</span>\n\t</p>\n<!-- End Export citation and abstract tools -->\n<!-- Start AAS Related Links -->\n<div class=\"reveal-container reveal-closed reveal-plus-icon bdt-1 mt-2 mb-2 wd-jnl-art-article-aas-related-links\">\n  <div class=\"replica-h3 mt-0\">\n    <a href=\"#\" class=\"reveal-trigger\" data-reveal-label-alt=\"Hide related links\">Related links</a>\n  </div>\n  <div class=\"reveal-content\">\n    <div class=\"article-meta\"> \n      <ul>\n      \t<li><a href=\"http://adsabs.harvard.edu/abs/2017ApJ...834..210C\" target=\"_blank\">NASA ADS Record <span class=\"icon-newtab\"></span></a></li>\n      \t<li><a href=\"http://nedwww.ipac.caltech.edu/cgi-bin/nph-objsearch?refcode=2017ApJ...834..210C&search_type=Search \" target=\"_blank\">NED Objects <span class=\"icon-newtab\"></span></a></li>\n      \t<li><a href=\"http://simbad.u-strasbg.fr/simbad/sim-ref?querymethod=bib&simbo=on&bibcode=2017ApJ...834..210C\" target=\"_blank\">Simbad Objects <span class=\"icon-newtab\"></span></a></li>\n      \t<li><a href=\"/journal/0004-637X/page/About%20Related%20Links\" target=\"_blank\">About Related Links</a></li>\n      \t</ul>\n    </div>\n  </div>\n</div>\n<!-- Start info bottom -->\n    <!-- End info bottom -->\n<!-- Start article full text -->\n\t<div xmlns:book=\"http://api.iop.org/Book/1.0/\" itemprop=\"articleBody\" class=\"wd-jnl-art-full-text article-text\">\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjaa3c3es1\" name=\"apjaa3c3es1\">1.\u00a0INTRODUCTION</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>Galaxy clusters have long been considered one of the most important cosmological probes due to their privileged position in the hierarchical formation scenario as the most massive and virialized objects. As such, galaxy clusters can inform important tests of the standard concordance cosmological model, and provide unique laboratories for the understanding of dark matter (DM) properties (see Voit <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib80\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib80\">2005</a>, for a review). For example, the number density of galaxy clusters is sensitive to the amplitude of the primordial density fluctuations, and can therefore be used as a powerful tool for estimating cosmological parameters, so long as the masses and redshifts of clusters are measured with good precision (Evrard et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib34\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib34\">2002</a>; Vikhlinin et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib78\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib78\">2009</a>; Rozo et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib67\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib67\">2010</a>).</p><p>In recent years, extensive efforts have been made to determine the mass and redshift distribution of samples of galaxy clusters (B\u00f6hringer et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib16\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib16\">2004</a>; Gladders &amp; Yee <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib43\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib43\">2005</a>; Burenin et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib19\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib19\">2007</a>; Gilbank et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib39\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib39\">2011</a>; Bleem et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib15\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib15\">2015</a>). Most of these mass measurements are based on baryonic signatures\u2014e.g., optical light, X-ray emission, and the Sunyaev Zel'dovich effect (SZe). However, there are pernicious systematic uncertainties that affect these baryonic signatures, which trace only a small fraction of the total matter content of galaxy clusters. The most robust and direct way to map the total (baryonic and dark) matter distribution in galaxy clusters is through the analysis of gravitational lensing effects, whereby background galaxy profiles are modified by the gravitational potential of foreground clusters, producing shape distortions and apparent flux magnification (see reviews by Bartelmann <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib5\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib5\">2010</a>\u00a0and\u00a0Kneib &amp; Natarajan <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib56\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib56\">2011</a>).</p><p>In the outer regions of galaxy clusters, the gravitational potential produces only tiny distortions that do not constrain the cluster profile individually, and therefore it is necessary to perform statistical measurements of the distortions affecting large ensembles of background galaxies. This is the so-called weak lensing (WL) regime, and the application of WL measurements has become a powerful tool in the recent years (Dahle <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib25\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib25\">2006</a>; Hoekstra &amp; Jain <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib48\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib48\">2008</a>; Okabe et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib63\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib63\">2010</a>; High et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib47\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib47\">2012</a>; Umetsu et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib76\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib76\">2014</a>; van Uitert et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib77\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib77\">2015</a>). In the innermost region of clusters\u2014the strong lensing (SL) regime\u2014the mass density can be high enough to produce dramatic distortions in the form of giant elongated arcs or multiple images of background sources, which can inform high-precision mass reconstruction studies of the cluster core (e.g., Zitrin et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib92\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib92\">2009</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib93\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib93\">2012a</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib94\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib94\">2012b</a>; Limousin et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib59\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib59\">2012</a>). Statistical analysis of samples of galaxy clusters that exhibit the hallmarks\u2014giant arcs and multiply imaged sources\u2014of strong lensing can also be used in tests of the \u039bCDM cosmological model. For example, several different studies comparing observations and predictions for the abundance of giant arcs (\"giant arc statistics\") find that simulations under-predict the number of giant arcs on the sky by perhaps as much as an order of magnitude (Bartelmann et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib6\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib6\">1998</a>; Gladders et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib41\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib41\">2003</a>; Li et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib58\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib58\">2006</a>).</p><p>In addition to their cosmological applications, the large magnification effects that typically act on strongly lensed background galaxies allows for\u00a0the study of high-redshift galaxies, which would otherwise be too faint to be observed. However, cases of bright, high-magnification strong lensing remain relatively rare phenomena, occurring in only a\u00a0small fraction of galaxy clusters (Horesh et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib49\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib49\">2010</a>). It is therefore always valuable to identify and follow-up on new samples of strong lensing galaxy clusters. Identifying new strong lensing samples serves to effectively increase the volume of the early universe that is available for observations (e.g., Bradley et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib18\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib18\">2008</a>; Bouwens et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib17\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib17\">2009</a>; Zheng et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib90\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib90\">2009</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib91\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib91\">2012</a>; Wuyts et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib86\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib86\">2010</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib87\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib87\">2014</a>; Bayliss et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib10\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib10\">2011b</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib11\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib11\">2010</a>; Coe et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib23\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib23\">2013</a>).</p><p>In this paper, we present the results of a spectroscopic follow-up campaign of 29 SL-selected galaxy clusters with 6.5\u20138.2 m class telescopes, discovered primarily in the Second Red-Sequence Cluster Survey (RCS-2; Gilbank et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib39\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib39\">2011</a>). The data presented in this work reveal the nature of the giant arc and multiple-image candidates, increasing the number of spectroscopically confirmed lensed galaxies at high-redshift. From these data, we also identify redshifts for more than one thousand cluster member galaxies, which are used to estimate dynamical masses of these clusters. The combination of these results will allow us to derive robust reconstructions of the matter distribution in these galaxy clusters.</p><p>This paper is organized as follows. In Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaa3c3es2\">2,</a> we describe the cluster sample and summarize the spectroscopic follow-up campaign. In Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaa3c3es3\">3,</a> we present the redshift results for the lensed galaxies and cluster members, as well as the final redshift and velocity dispersion of the clusters. In Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaa3c3es4\">4,</a> we show the dynamical mass results, while, in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaa3c3es5\">5,</a>\u00a0we analyze the possible systematics that might affect our measurements. Lastly, we summarize the main results and present the final conclusions in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaa3c3es6\">6</a>. Throughout the paper, we assume a flat \u039bCDM cosmology with \u03a9<sub><em>m</em></sub>\u00a0=\u00a00.27, \u03a9<sub>\u039b</sub>\u00a0=\u00a00.73, and <em>H</em><sub>0</sub>\u00a0=\u00a070 <em>h</em><sub>70</sub> km s<sup>\u22121</sup> Mpc<sup>\u22121</sup>.</p></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjaa3c3es2\" name=\"apjaa3c3es2\">2.\u00a0OBSERVATIONS</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><h3 id=\"apjaa3c3es2-1\" name=\"apjaa3c3es2-1\">2.1.\u00a0The SL-selected Cluster Sample</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>The cluster sample presented here is a subset of a larger sample of more than one hundred SL-selected clusters that have been identified primarily in RCS-2 imaging data. The median seeing of the RCS-2 survey is ~0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />7, making it ideal for the detection and classification of giant arcs. Furthermore, the RCS-2 survey was designed to detect galaxy clusters out to <em>z</em>\u00a0~\u00a01.1 using the cluster red-sequence technique (Gladders &amp; Yee <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib42\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib42\">2000</a>) in deep wide-field images. We have searched the resulting galaxy cluster catalog for SL systems, where the SL selection criterion is the presence of one or more giant arcs around a galaxy cluster's core. The SL-selected clusters identified in the RCS-2 comprise a new sample of giant arcs, the Red-Sequence Cluster Survey Giant Arc (RCSGA; Bayliss <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib8\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib8\">2012</a>; M. D. Gladders et al. 2016, in preparation) catalog. Giant arc candidates are flagged by multiple members of our team performing independent visual inspections of the entire RCS-2 red-sequence catalog of galaxy clusters, and systems that are flagged by most or all inspectors constitute the SL cluster sample, as described in more detail by Bayliss (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib8\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib8\">2012</a>). We supplement the RCS-2 SL-selected cluster sample with a few systems chosen to fill R.A. gaps that were similarly selected from the Sloan Digital Sky Survey (SDSS; York et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib89\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib89\">2000</a>).</p><p>We performed a comprehensive spectroscopic follow-up of 29 SL-selected galaxy clusters primarily from the RCSGA; 7 of these clusters were previously unpublished. The cluster sample is presented in Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjaa3c3et1\">1</a>, where clusters formerly reported in other studies are marked with their corresponding references. We have adopted the naming convention described in Bayliss et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib10\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib10\">2011b</a>) for giant arcs discovered in RCSGA, given by RCSGA\u00a0\u2212\u00a0Jhhmmss+ddmmss (e.g., Wuyts et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib86\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib86\">2010</a>; Bayliss <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib8\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib8\">2012</a>).</p><div tabindex=\"-1\" id=\"apjaa3c3et1\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\"><p><b>Table 1.</b>\u00a0\nSummary of VLT/<em>Magellan</em> Spectroscopic Observations\n</p><table cellpadding=\"0\" cellspacing=\"0\" border=\"0\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjaa3c3et1\" data-toolbar-img=\"/0004-637X/834/2/210/suppdata/apjaa3c3et1_lr.gif\" data-toolbar-type=\"table\" data-toolbar-title=\"Table 1\"><colgroup><col align=\"left\" char=\"\"></col><col align=\"left\" char=\"\"></col><col align=\"left\" char=\"\"></col><col align=\"left\" char=\"\"></col><col align=\"left\" char=\"\"></col><col align=\"left\" char=\"\"></col></colgroup><thead>\n<tr valign=\"top\">\n<th scope=\"col\" colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"left\">\u00a0</th>\n<th scope=\"col\" colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">\u00a0</th>\n<th scope=\"col\" colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">\u00a0</th>\n<th scope=\"col\" colspan=\"2\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">Total Exposure Time</th>\n<th scope=\"col\" colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">\u00a0</th>\n</tr>\n<tr valign=\"top\">\n<th scope=\"col\" colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"left\">Target</th>\n<th scope=\"col\" colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">R.A.<sup>a</sup>\n</th>\n<th scope=\"col\" colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">Decl.<sup>a</sup>\n</th>\n<th scope=\"col\" colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">FORS2/VLT</th>\n<th scope=\"col\" colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">IMACS/<em>Magellan</em></th>\n<th scope=\"col\" colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">Prev. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib79\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib79\">1999</a>)</td>\n</tr>\n<tr valign=\"top\">\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"left\">SDSS J2313\u22120104</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"char\" char=\".\">23 13 54.514</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"char\" char=\".\">\u221201 04 48.46</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"char\" char=\".\">1.50/2</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">1.17/1</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">Geach et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib37\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib37\">2011</a>)</td>\n</tr>\n<tr valign=\"top\">\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"left\">RCS2 J2329\u22121317</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"char\" char=\".\">23 29 09.528</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"char\" char=\".\">\u221213 17 49.26</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"char\" char=\".\">4.08/4</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">1.33/1</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">...</td>\n</tr>\n<tr valign=\"top\">\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"left\">RCS2 J2329\u22120120</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"char\" char=\".\">23 29 47.782</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"char\" char=\".\">\u221201 20 46.89</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"char\" char=\".\">2.00/1</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">2.00/1</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">White et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib83\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib83\">1997</a>)</td>\n</tr>\n<tr valign=\"top\">\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"left\">RCS2 J2336\u22120608</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"char\" char=\".\">23 36 20.838</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"char\" char=\".\">\u221206 08 35.81</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"char\" char=\".\">3.00/2</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">...</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">Wen et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib81b\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib81b\">2012</a>)</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody></table><p><small>\n<p><strong>Notes.</strong></p>\n<a name=\"apjaa3c3efn4\" id=\"apjaa3c3efn4\"></a><sup> a</sup>Coordinates correspond to the BCG centroids in sexagesimal degrees (J2000).\n<a name=\"apjaa3c3efn5\" id=\"apjaa3c3efn5\"></a><sup> b</sup>Total integration time for lensed galaxy candidates (for instrument/telescope), distributed across the number of masks listed in the spectroscopic follow-up. Slits targeting\u00a0cluster members varied between each mask; therefore, the total integration time shown in this table does not necessarily correspond to the total integration time of these objects.\n<a name=\"apjaa3c3efn6\" id=\"apjaa3c3efn6\"></a><sup> c</sup>Clusters formerly identified and/or described in previous studies.\n<a name=\"apjaa3c3efn7\" id=\"apjaa3c3efn7\"></a><sup> d</sup>Clusters reported in the <em>ROSAT</em> all-sky bright source catalog (Voges et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib79\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib79\">1999</a>) as RX J0034.4+0225 and RX J2151.4\u22120139, respectively.\n</small></p><p class=\"franklin fs-16\">\n\t\t\tDownload table as:\u00a0\n            <span class=\"btn-multi-block\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary wd-jnl-art-btn-ascii\" href=\"/0004-637X/834/2/210/suppdata/apjaa3c3et1_ascii.txt?doi=10.3847/1538-4357/834/2/210\" target=\"_blank\">ASCII</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary wd-jnl-art-btn-typeset typeset-table-img\" href=\"/0004-637X/834/2/210/suppdata/apjaa3c3et1_lr.gif\" target=\"_blank\">Typeset image</a></span></p></div></div><h3 id=\"apjaa3c3es2-2\" name=\"apjaa3c3es2-2\">2.2.\u00a0Imaging</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>Most of the imaging data presented here have been obtained from the RCS-2 survey. They were collected in queue-scheduled mode with MegaCam at the 3.6 m Canada\u2013France\u2013Hawaii Telescope (CFHT), between the semesters 2003A and 2007B inclusive. The RCS-2 data consist of\u00a0single exposures (without dithering) of 4, 8, and 6 minutes, for the <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/834/2/210/Full/apjaa3c3eieqn2.gif\" alt=\"$g^{\\prime} ,r^{\\prime} ,$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">g^{\\prime} ,r^{\\prime} ,</script></span></span> and <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/834/2/210/Full/apjaa3c3eieqn3.gif\" alt=\"$z^{\\prime} $\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">z^{\\prime}</script></span></span> bands, yielding 5<em>\u03c3</em> limiting magnitudes of 24.3, 24.4, and 22.8, respectively (Gilbank et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib39\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib39\">2011</a>). We used the <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/834/2/210/Full/apjaa3c3eieqn4.gif\" alt=\"$r^{\\prime} $\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">r^{\\prime}</script></span></span> and <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/834/2/210/Full/apjaa3c3eieqn5.gif\" alt=\"$z^{\\prime} $\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">z^{\\prime}</script></span></span> bands to identify the red-sequence cluster member candidates, which have been used as targets for the spectroscopic follow-up. Color images from all three bands were used to identify strongly lensed arcs. We have also obtained pre-imaging of our clusters in <em>B</em>, <em>R</em>, and <em>I</em> bands, with the Focal Reducer and low dispersion Spectrograph 2 (FORS2; Appenzeller et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib4\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib4\">1998</a>) at the ESO<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjaa3c3efn8\">12</a></sup>\n 8.2 m Very Large Telescope (VLT), in queue mode. These pre-imaging data have mainly\u00a0been\u00a0used to design the spectroscopic masks but they also help in the search of multiple-image systems.</p></div><h3 id=\"apjaa3c3es2-3\" name=\"apjaa3c3es2-3\">2.3.\u00a0Spectroscopy and Data Reduction</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><h4 id=\"apjaa3c3es2-3-1\" name=\"apjaa3c3es2-3-1\">2.3.1.\u00a0FORS2/VLT</h4><div class=\"article-text\"><p>The FORS2/VLT observations were carried out between 2006 October and 2010 March using the Multi-object spectroscopy with exchangeable masks (MXU) mode. The MXU mode allows one both to increase the density of the slits and to freely manipulate the width, length, and orientation of the slits, making it ideal for spectroscopy in dense regions like the cluster cores. The masks were strategically positioned in the center of each cluster in order to prioritize giant arcs and lensed galaxy candidates. The central slit widths were set to 1'', while their length was varied depending on the arc candidate size, typically between 12'' and 25''. Subsequently, the masks were filled in with slits placed on red-sequence selected cluster members, prioritizing brighter galaxies ahead of fainter ones. The length of these slits was set between 6'' and 12'', depending on each member candidate size. Two masks were usually constructed for each cluster, though some of them had up to four. In order to increase the flux of our lensed galaxy candidates, we fixed the slits located at the arc positions. On the other hand, with the purpose of increasing the number of spectroscopically confirmed cluster members, we varied the slits located on member candidates between each mask. We used a low spectral resolution instrument setup by combining the GRIS_150I+27 grism and GG435+81 filter, and adopted a 2\u00a0<b>&#x00d7;</b>\u00a02 binning in order to improve the signal-to-noise ratio of the spectra. This configuration results in a final dispersion of 6.9 \u00c5 per image pixel, and covers a spectral range of \u0394<em>\u03bb</em>\u00a0~\u00a04300\u201310500 \u00c5, with our highest sensitivity in the interval \u0394<em>\u03bb</em>\u00a0~\u00a04500\u20139000 \u00c5, due to the transmission efficiency of the filter used, as well as to the detector's quantum efficiency. Depending on the intrinsic features of each target, we varied the exposure times between 1200 and 3000 s. The total integration time for each galaxy cluster is reported in Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjaa3c3et1\">1</a>.</p><p>The basic data reduction steps consisted of bias subtraction, flat-fielding, wavelength calibration, and sky subtraction. These steps were carried out by using the ESO Recipe Execution Tool (EsoRex<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjaa3c3efn9\">13</a></sup>\n) and the Common Pipeline Library (CPL<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjaa3c3efn10\">14</a></sup>\n). The wavelength calibration was done by comparison to the standard He+Ne+Ar lamp observations. Due to the nature of giant arcs, long and elongated objects, the sky subtraction was a complicated task and was carried out independently. The other advanced steps consisted of the removal of cosmic rays, the 1D spectra extraction, and the average of multiple spectra for each source. These steps were performed using our own IDL routines, inspired by the optimal extraction algorithm by Horne (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib50\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib50\">1986</a>).</p></div><h4 id=\"apjaa3c3es2-3-2\" name=\"apjaa3c3es2-3-2\">2.3.2.\u00a0IMACS/Magellan</h4><div class=\"article-text\"><p>We have also performed spectroscopic observations with the Inamori <em>Magellan</em> Areal Camera and Spectrograph (IMACS; Dressler et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib30\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib30\">2006</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib29\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib29\">2011</a>) on the 6.5 m <em>Magellan</em> (Baade) telescope at LCO.<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjaa3c3efn11\">15</a></sup>\n The IMACS/<em>Magellan</em> observations were collected during six\u00a0different runs between 2008 June and 2011 March. We used the Gladders Image-Slicing Multislit Option (GISMO<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjaa3c3efn12\">16</a></sup>\n) at IMACS, which allows high-efficiency spectroscopy for galaxy clusters, obtaining spectra from ~20\u201360 members in a single spectroscopic mask. The mask design was performed following the same strategy described above, though the slit length was considerably shorter than the VLT data. The GISMO-IMACS masks had slits that were consistently ~5<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />5\u20136<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />5 long. The IMACS <em>f</em>/4 camera and the grating of 300 lines/mm were used to obtain 2\u00a0<b>&#x00d7;</b>\u00a02 binning spectra, covering an effective spectral range of \u0394<em>\u03bb</em>\u00a0~\u00a04800\u20138000 \u00c5, with a final dispersion of ~1.5 \u00c5 per image pixel. The exposure time of the GISMO-IMACS observations varied between 2400\u20139600 s. The total integration time for each cluster is reported in Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjaa3c3et1\">1</a>.</p><p>The GISMO-IMACS data were reduced using the Carnegie Observatories System for MultiObject Spectroscopy (COSMOS<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjaa3c3efn13\">17</a></sup>\n) pipeline. The COSMOS reduction process consisted of bias subtraction, flat-fielding, and a wavelength calibration using comparison arcs. The wavelength solutions have typical rms <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/lesssim.gif\" alt=\"lesssim\" align=\"baseline\" />0.5 \u00c5. The sky subtraction was carried out by constructing a cosmic-ray-cleaned median sky spectrum for each slit, which was then subtracted from the slit image. Finally, the 1D spectra extraction and the combination of different exposures were performed using our own IDL routines.</p></div></div></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjaa3c3es3\" name=\"apjaa3c3es3\">3.\u00a0RESULTS</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><h3 id=\"apjaa3c3es3-1\" name=\"apjaa3c3es3-1\">3.1.\u00a0Redshift Measurements</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>The spectroscopic redshifts were determined with two independent methods for <em>Magellan</em> and VLT data. In the first procedure, galaxy redshifts were measured by cross-correlating the spectra with galaxy spectral templates of the SDSS Data Release 7 (DR7; Abazajian et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib1\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib1\">2009</a>) using the RVSAO/XCSAO package for IRAF<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjaa3c3efn14\">18</a></sup>\n (Kurtz &amp; Mink <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib57\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib57\">1998</a>). The spectral features in each spectrum were confirmed by visual inspection with the 2D spectra. This technique yielded accurate redshift measurements for high/medium signal-to-noise spectra. However, most of the lensed galaxies have low signal-to-noise ratios, and thus, the redshift results of these cross-correlations have low reliability. In order to determine reliable spectroscopic redshift for all lensed galaxies, we used a second method; we assigned redshifts to individual spectra by identifying a set of lines at a common redshift, fitting a Gaussian profile to each line in order to determine their central wavelength, and taking the mean redshift of the entire set of lines. The sets of emission and absorption lines used in the redshift measurements of the lensed galaxies varied significantly among the different source spectra (Bayliss et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib10\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib10\">2011b</a>). Most emission lines observed in the giant arc spectra coincided with C <span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>] <em>\u03bb</em>1908.73 \u00c5, [O <span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span>] <em>\u03bb</em>3727.09, 3729.88 \u00c5, H<em>\u03b2 \u03bb</em>4862.68 \u00c5, [O <span class=\"small-caps\">iii</span>] <em>\u03bb</em>4960.30, and 5008.24 \u00c5, which usually correspond to emission lines that come from star forming regions, matching the expectations for high-redshift blue galaxies. Due to the spectral range covered by our instrument setups, these emission lines are observed only in lensed galaxies at <em>z</em>\u00a0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/lesssim.gif\" alt=\"lesssim\" align=\"baseline\" />\u00a01.7. For background sources at higher redshifts, we had to rely on the rest-frame UV features to determine their redshifts. The most common UV metal absorption lines observed in the lensed galaxy spectra were Mg <span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span> <em>\u03bb</em>2796.35, 2803.53 \u00c5, Mg <span class=\"small-caps\">i</span> <em>\u03bb</em>2852.96 \u00c5, Fe <span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span> <em>\u03bb</em>2344.21, 2374.46, 2382.76, 2586.65, 2600.17 \u00c5, C <span class=\"small-caps\">iv</span> <em>\u03bb</em>1548.20, 1550.78 \u00c5, Si <span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span> <em>\u03bb</em>1260.42, 1304.37, 1526.71 \u00c5, and Si <span class=\"small-caps\">iv</span> <em>\u03bb</em>1393.76, 1402.77 \u00c5. For completeness, this second method was also applied to the rest of the spectra in our sample. In this case, the redshift measurements of cluster member galaxies were derived from at least three lines;\u00a0most used ones\u00a0that\u00a0corresponded to the characteristic lines in older stellar populations (e.g., Ca <span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span> H&amp;K <em>\u03bb</em>3934.77, 3969.59 \u00c5, g-band <em>\u03bb</em>4305.61 \u00c5, Mg <span class=\"small-caps\">i</span> <em>\u03bb</em>5168.74, 5174.14, 5185.04 \u00c5, and Na <span class=\"small-caps\">i</span> <em>\u03bb</em>5891.61, 5894.13, 5897.57 \u00c5), though some emission lines were also observed several times (e.g., [O <span class=\"small-caps\">ii</span>] <em>\u03bb</em>3727.09, 3729.88 \u00c5). The spectral lines used in this analysis were taken from Shapley et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib71\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib71\">2003</a>), Erb et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib32\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib32\">2012</a>), and also from the SDSS spectral line tables.<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjaa3c3efn15\">19</a></sup>\n It should be noted that all vacuum wavelengths were converted to air wavelengths by applying the IAU standard conversion (Morton <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib61\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib61\">1991</a>), in order to measure redshifts in the air frame.</p><p>Redshift errors are mainly due to the combination of the uncertainty in our wavelength calibrations and the statistical uncertainty in the identification of line centers. The median rms in the wavelength calibration is ~1.4 \u00c5, which at a central wavelength of ~7000 \u00c5, results in redshift errors of ~\u00b10.0002. The redshift errors for the high signal-to-noise spectra are distributed around ~\u00b10.0005. These errors are in agreement with the expected ones, since we have to add the uncertainties in the line center identifications. However, the errors increase for low signal-to-noise spectra, assuming values of the order of ~\u00b10.001.</p><p>Following the work done by Bayliss et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib10\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib10\">2011b</a>), we classified our redshift measurements into four classes, which describe the confidence level of the redshift measurements. Class 3 redshifts are the highest confidence measurements, typically obtained from more than four\u00a0absorption and/or emission features. These redshift measurements are secure. Given the high signal-to-noise ratio of the cluster member spectra, virtually all of\u00a0their redshift measurements fall into this category. Approximately 55% of the redshift estimates of the lensed galaxies are also classified as class 3 spectra. Five examples of class 3 lensed galaxy spectra are shown in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ef1\">1</a>. Class 2 redshifts are medium-confidence measurements. These estimates are based on at least two prominent lines and/or a larger number of low-significance features. The redshifts with this classification are very likely to be the real redshifts of the corresponding spectra, but there is a small chance that some of them could have been misidentified. Of the order of 21% of the lensed galaxy spectra fall into this classification. Two examples of class 2 lensed galaxy spectra are shown in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ef2\">2</a>. Class 1 redshifts are low-confidence measurements, which are based on a\u00a0few low-significance spectral features and represent the \"best-guess\" redshift using the available spectral data. Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ef3\">3</a> shows two examples of class 1 lensed galaxy spectra. Redshift estimates falling in this category correspond to &lt;23% of all lensed galaxy spectra. Finally, we have the class 0 redshift for those cases where the spectral analysis shows no evidence of spectral features. There is only one lensed galaxy spectrum in our sample where the redshift measurement completely failed.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjaa3c3ef1\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjaa3c3ef1\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/834/2/210/Full/apjaa3c3ef1_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 1.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 1.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/834/2/210/Full/apjaa3c3ef1_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 1.</strong>\u00a0FORS2/VLT spectra for five lensed galaxies with highly reliable redshift measurements, labeled as class 3. These lensed galaxies are distributed in a wide redshift range from 0.8\u00a0\u2264\u00a0<em>z</em>\u00a0\u2264\u00a02.9, with a median redshift of <em>z</em><sub><em>s</em></sub>\u00a0=\u00a01.8\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.1, which is consistent with the spectroscopic and color analysis of high-redshift lensed galaxies performed by Bayliss et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib9\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib9\">2011a</a>) and Bayliss (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib8\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib8\">2012</a>) for hundreds of giant arcs identified in the RCS-2 and SDSS surveys. It should be noted that &gt;75% of the spectra have a confidence level in their redshift measurements \u22652. The redshift distribution of our lensed galaxy sample and the median redshift found by these previous works are shown in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ef4\">4</a>. The redshift measurements of all confirmed lensed galaxies are reported in Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjaa3c3et2\">2</a>, with labels that correspond to the label markers in Figures <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ef5\">5</a>\u2013<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ef12\">12</a>.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjaa3c3ef4\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjaa3c3ef4\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/834/2/210/Full/apjaa3c3ef4_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 4.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 4.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/834/2/210/Full/apjaa3c3ef4_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 4.</strong>\u00a0Redshift distribution of the lensed galaxy sample presented in this work. The blue dashed line corresponds to the median redshift of our sample, <em>z</em><sub><em>s</em></sub>\u00a0=\u00a01.8\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.1, while the red solid line corresponds to the median redshift found by the spectroscopic and color analysis of high-redshift lensed galaxies\u2014primarily the larger full RCSGA sample\u2014by Bayliss (<a class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib8\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib8\">2012</a>).</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" valign=\"top\">\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"left\">...</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"char\" char=\".\">S1.2</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"char\" char=\".\">23 36 20.38</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"char\" char=\".\">\u221206 08 33.8</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"char\" char=\".\">1.295</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"char\" char=\".\">1</td>\n</tr>\n</tbody></table><p><small>\n<p><strong>Notes.</strong></p>\n<a name=\"apjaa3c3efn16\" id=\"apjaa3c3efn16\"></a><sup> a</sup>Lensed galaxy labels that correspond to the label markers in Figures <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ef5\">5</a>\u2013<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ef12\">12</a>. S1.X spectra correspond to the \"primary\" arcs of each cluster, following the notation in Bayliss et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib10\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib10\">2011b</a>).\n<a name=\"apjaa3c3efn17\" id=\"apjaa3c3efn17\"></a><sup> b</sup>Coordinates of the lensed galaxies in sexagesimal degrees (J2000).\n<a name=\"apjaa3c3efn18\" id=\"apjaa3c3efn18\"></a><sup> c</sup>Spectroscopic redshift.\n<a name=\"apjaa3c3efn19\" id=\"apjaa3c3efn19\"></a><sup> d</sup>Classification of the redshift measurements, as discussed in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaa3c3es3-1\">3.1</a>.\n</small></p><p class=\"franklin fs-16\">\n\t\t\tDownload table as:\u00a0\n            <span class=\"btn-multi-block\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary wd-jnl-art-btn-ascii\" href=\"/0004-637X/834/2/210/suppdata/apjaa3c3et2_ascii.txt?doi=10.3847/1538-4357/834/2/210\" target=\"_blank\">ASCII</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary wd-jnl-art-btn-typeset typeset-table-img\" href=\"/0004-637X/834/2/210/suppdata/apjaa3c3et2_lr.gif\" target=\"_blank\">Typeset image</a></span></p></div><p>Furthermore, this data\u00a0set extends the number of galaxy clusters with spectroscopic confirmation of their SL features available to perform lensing reconstructions of their mass distribution, especially at <em>z</em> <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/gsim.gif\" alt=\"gsim\" align=\"baseline\" /> 0.2.</p></div><h3 id=\"apjaa3c3es3-3\" name=\"apjaa3c3es3-3\">3.3.\u00a0Cluster Redshifts and Velocity Dispersions</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>The correct determination of cluster members is crucial to avoid biases in the velocity dispersion and mass measurements (Beers et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib12\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib12\">1990</a>; Ruel et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib68\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib68\">2014</a>). Our selection method of cluster members is an iterative process that starts by applying a cut in the (rest-frame) velocity space of 4000 km s<sup>\u22121</sup>, centered at the median redshift of all candidates. Then, the 3<em>\u03c3</em> clipping method is applied to remove the interlopers and the median redshift is recomputed. This process is iterated until the number of members is stable, which usually occurs after the second or third iteration. We have checked possible systematic effects of this procedure by applying the shifting gapper method (Fadda et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib35\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib35\">1996</a>) to those clusters with a large number of galaxies falling into the \u00b14000 km s<sup>\u22121</sup>. The results in both methods are fully consistent.</p><p>From this analysis, we have recovered a total of 1004 spectroscopically confirmed clusters, that result\u00a0in an average of <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/834/2/210/Full/apjaa3c3eieqn6.gif\" alt=\"$\\langle {N}_{{\\rm{mem}}}\\rangle \\sim 35$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">\\langle {N}_{{\\rm{mem}}}\\rangle \\sim 35</script></span></span> member galaxies per cluster. The spectroscopic redshift information of all cluster members reported in this work is available as supplementary material in the machine-readable format at the Astrophysical Journal. A portion is shown in Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjaa3c3et3\">3</a> for guidance regarding its form and content. These data have been used to compute robust measurements of redshift and velocity dispersion of our SL-selected galaxy clusters by applying the bi-weight estimator for robust statistics (Beers et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib12\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib12\">1990</a>). The errors on redshift and velocity dispersion of each cluster were estimated through many bootstrapped realizations, identifying the upper and lower 68% confidence intervals. It should be noted that the errors of individual galaxy redshifts were not considered in the velocity dispersion estimates, because the bias introduced by this exclusion is &lt;0.1% for massive clusters (Danese et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib26\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib26\">1980</a>), as in our sample.</p><div tabindex=\"-1\" id=\"apjaa3c3et3\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\"><p><b>Table 3.</b>\u00a0\nThe VLT/<em>Magellan</em> Spectroscopic Redshift Catalog of the Cluster Members Reported in This a</sup>Coordinates are BCG centroids in sexagesimal degrees (J2000).\n<a name=\"apjaa3c3efn24\" id=\"apjaa3c3efn24\"></a><sup> b</sup>The rest-frame velocity dispersion of the clusters.\n<a name=\"apjaa3c3efn25\" id=\"apjaa3c3efn25\"></a><sup> c</sup><em>P</em>-values of the DS test. The uncertainties are given by the upper and lower 68% confidence intervals. The threshold for substructure detection is set to an s.l. of 0.05 within uncertainties.\n<a name=\"apjaa3c3efn26\" id=\"apjaa3c3efn26\"></a><sup> d</sup><em>P</em>-values of the KS test. The uncertainties are given by the upper and lower 68% confidence intervals. The threshold for rejecting the null hypothesis is set to an s.l. of 0.05 within uncertainties.\n<a name=\"apjaa3c3efn27\" id=\"apjaa3c3efn27\"></a><sup> e</sup>Systems with few spectroscopically confirmed cluster members, <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/834/2/210/Full/apjaa3c3eieqn57.gif\" alt=\"${N}_{{\\rm{mem}}}\\lesssim 20$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{N}_{{\\rm{mem}}}\\lesssim 20</script></span></span>. Their velocity dispersion and mass measurements represent only a first guess and should not be considered as the final estimates.\n<a name=\"apjaa3c3efn28\" id=\"apjaa3c3efn28\"></a><sup> f</sup>Systems showing signs of dynamical substructure based on DS test results.\n<a name=\"apjaa3c3efn29\" id=\"apjaa3c3efn29\"></a><sup> g</sup>The null hypothesis of the KS test is rejected, suggesting that the velocity data of this system is not consistent with a uni-modal distribution. Its velocity dispersion and mass measurements represent only a first guess and should not be considered as the final estimates.\n</small></p><p class=\"franklin fs-16\">\n\t\t\tDownload table as:\u00a0\n            <span class=\"btn-multi-block\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary wd-jnl-art-btn-ascii\" href=\"/0004-637X/834/2/210/suppdata/apjaa3c3et4_ascii.txt?doi=10.3847/1538-4357/834/2/210\" target=\"_blank\">ASCII</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary wd-jnl-art-btn-typeset typeset-table-img\" href=\"/0004-637X/834/2/210/suppdata/apjaa3c3et4_lr.gif\" target=\"_blank\">Typeset image</a></span></p></div></div></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjaa3c3es4\" name=\"apjaa3c3es4\">4.\u00a0DYNAMICAL MASSES</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>The dynamical information of galaxy clusters offers a\u00a0unique possibility for estimating the virial mass of these systems through the relationship between the velocity dispersion of galaxy members and the cluster mass. Mass estimates based on simple variations of the virial theorem are biased high by a factor of 10%\u201320% compared with masses obtained from the Jeans analysis (Carlberg et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib21\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib21\">1997</a>) and the caustic technique (Diaferio &amp; Geller <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib27\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib27\">1997</a>). In order to account for this bias and obtain a\u00a0universal virial scaling relation for massive DM halos, Evrard et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib33\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib33\">2008</a>) studied an ensemble of cold DM simulations in a variety of cosmologies. They concluded that the large majority (~90%) of massive halos (<em>M</em><sub>200</sub>\u00a0\u2265\u00a010<sup>14</sup> <em>M</em><sub>\u2299</sub>) are, on average, and in all cosmologies, consistent with a virialized state and obey a power-law relation between one-dimensional DM particle velocity dispersion, <em>\u03c3</em><sub>DM</sub>, and halo mass. Accordingly, the mass enclosed within the virial radius, <em>r</em><sub>200</sub>, scales as</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"apjaa3c3eeqn1\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/834/2/210/Full/apjaa3c3eeqn1.gif\" alt=\"Equation (1)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray}&&{M}_{200}=\\displaystyle \\frac{{10}^{15}}{h(z)}{\\left(\\displaystyle \\frac{{\\sigma }_{\\mathrm{DM}}}{{\\sigma }_{15}}\\right)}^{1/\\alpha }{M}_{\\odot },\\end{eqnarray}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\t1\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>where <em>\u03c3</em><sub>15</sub>\u00a0=\u00a01082.9\u00a0\u00b1\u00a04.0 km s<sup>\u22121</sup> is the normalization for a halo mass of <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/834/2/210/Full/apjaa3c3eieqn58.gif\" alt=\"${10}^{15}{h}^{-1}{M}_{\\odot }$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{10}^{15}{h}^{-1}{M}_{\\odot }</script></span></span>, <em>\u03b1</em>\u00a0=\u00a00.3361\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.0026 is the logarithmic slope, and <em>h</em>(<em>z</em>)\u00a0=\u00a0<em>H</em>(<em>z</em>)/ 100 km s<sup>\u22121</sup> Mpc<sup>\u22121</sup> is the normalized Hubble parameter at redshift <em>z</em> for a flat universe.</p><p>Computing dynamical mass from Equation (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"eqnref\" href=\"#apjaa3c3eeqn1\">1</a>) requires a good understanding of the relationship between the DM particle velocity dispersion of cluster halos and the velocity dispersion of cluster members, <em>\u03c3</em><sub><em>c</em></sub>, often parameterized by the velocity bias <em>b</em><sub><em>v</em></sub>\u00a0=\u00a0<em>\u03c3</em><sub><em>c</em></sub>/<em>\u03c3</em><sub>DM</sub>. Previous studies based on the simulation have shown that this velocity bias assumes values in the range of <em>b</em><sub><em>v</em></sub>\u00a0~\u00a01.0\u20131.3 (Col\u00edn et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib24\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib24\">2000</a>; Ghigna et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib38\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib38\">2000</a>; Diemand et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib28\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib28\">2004</a>), though more recent simulations have indicated that the way in which subhalos are tracked and defined affects the resulting velocity bias predictions (Evrard et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib33\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib33\">2008</a>; White et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib82\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib82\">2010</a>). In particular, subhalos that are treated in this way show no evidence of a possible velocity bias, i.e., <em>b</em><sub><em>v</em></sub>\u00a0~\u00a01.0. For consistency with these latest simulation results, we assume that galaxies are unbiased tracers of the total cluster mass (adopting <em>b</em><sub><em>v</em></sub>\u00a0=\u00a01), and derive dynamical masses of all clusters in our sample by applying Equation (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"eqnref\" href=\"#apjaa3c3eeqn1\">1</a>). The mass range of our SL sample goes from <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/834/2/210/Full/apjaa3c3eieqn59.gif\" alt=\"$5\\times {10}^{13}{h}_{70}^{-1}\\,{M}_{\\odot }$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">5\\times {10}^{13}{h}_{70}^{-1}\\,{M}_{\\odot }</script></span></span> to <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/834/2/210/Full/apjaa3c3eieqn60.gif\" alt=\"$1.9\\times {10}^{15}{h}_{70}^{-1}\\,{M}_{\\odot }$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">1.9\\times {10}^{15}{h}_{70}^{-1}\\,{M}_{\\odot }</script></span></span>. This wide mass range puts our sample in an excellent position to study relationships between the halo mass of galaxy clusters and their properties, such as the concentration, ellipticity, triaxiality, etc.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjaa3c3ef6\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjaa3c3ef6\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/834/2/210/Full/apjaa3c3ef6_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 6.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 6.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/834/2/210/Full/apjaa3c3ef6_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 6.</strong>\u00a0From the top-left to bottom-right panels, we show the SL-selected galaxy clusters RCS2 J0052+0433, RCS2 J0057+0209, RCS2 J0252\u22121459, and RCS2 J0309\u22121437. position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 9.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/834/2/210/Full/apjaa3c3ef9_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 9.</strong>\u00a0From the top-left to bottom-right panels, we show the SL-selected galaxy clusters RCS2 J1250+0244, RCS2 J1511+0630, SDSS J1517+1003, and SDSS J1519+0840. Lensed galaxies are labeled in the same manner as in Figure <a href=\"#apjaa3c3ef5\">5</a>. All images cover a field of view of 75''\u00a0<b>&#x00d7;</b>\u00a075''.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjaa3c3ef9\" href=\"/0004-637X/834/2/210/downloadFigure/figure/apjaa3c3ef9\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjaa3c3ef9\" href=\"/0004-637X/834/2/210/downloadHRFigure/figure/apjaa3c3ef9\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><p>The uncertainties in the mass estimates include both the systematic and statistical errors introduced by the <em>\u03c3</em><sub>DM</sub>\u00a0\u2212\u00a0<em>M</em><sub>200</sub> scaling relation, redshift measurements, and velocity dispersion estimates. The potential biases due to the lack of cluster members and line-of-sight projection together with other effects that might affect our measurements will be analyzed in the next section. The mass estimates and their respective errors are reported in the Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjaa3c3et4\">4</a>.</p></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjaa3c3es5\" name=\"apjaa3c3es5\">5.\u00a0DISCUSSION</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>Before we discuss the implications of these results, we explore some effects that may affect our measurements. The most important factors that might bias the results are the presence of substructure in our clusters, the region where cluster members were selected for the spectroscopic follow-up, and the final number of confirmed members used in the velocity dispersion estimates. The other biases are the result of assumptions made about the isotropy of galaxy orbits and the nature of the lensing cluster population itself.</p><h3 id=\"apjaa3c3es5-1\" name=\"apjaa3c3es5-1\">5.1.\u00a0Substructure</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>Numerical and observational studies have shown that a significant fraction of galaxy clusters contain substructure, which is frequently attributed to the active merging histories of massive halos (White et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib82\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib82\">2010</a>; Battaglia et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib7\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib7\">2012</a>). However, this does not affect the velocity dispersion measurements when the clusters under consideration have a uni-modal velocity distribution (Girardi et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib40\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib40\">1997</a>), i.e., when the substructure is only marginal and not of comparable size to the cluster itself (having &lt;10% of the cluster members in an average extension of ~0.2 Mpc h<sup>\u22121</sup>).</p><p>In order to search for substructure in our sample, we apply the DS test (Dressler &amp; Shectman <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib31\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib31\">1988</a>) to those clusters with high enough numbers of member galaxies (<em>N</em><sub>mem</sub> <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/gsim.gif\" alt=\"gsim\" align=\"baseline\" /> 20). The DS test has been proven to be\u00a0one of the most sensitive tests for dynamical substructure (Pinkney et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib65\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib65\">1996</a>) and widely used in the literature (Cen <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib22\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib22\">1997</a>; Knebe &amp; M\u00fcller <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib55\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib55\">2000</a>; Hou et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib51\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib51\">2009</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib52\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib52\">2012</a>; White et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib82\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib82\">2010</a>; Sif\u00f3n et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib72\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib72\">2013</a>). The test is based on the detection of localized subgroups of galaxies that deviate from the global distribution of velocities by using the substructure estimator <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/834/2/210/Full/apjaa3c3eieqn61.gif\" alt=\"${\\rm{\\Delta }}={\\sum }_{i}{\\delta }_{i}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{\\rm{\\Delta }}={\\sum }_{i}{\\delta }_{i}</script></span></span>, with</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"apjaa3c3eeqn2\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/834/2/210/Full/apjaa3c3eeqn2.gif\" alt=\"Equation (2)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray}&&{\\delta }_{i}^{2}=\\left(\\displaystyle \\frac{{N}_{{\\rm{local}}}+1}{{\\sigma }_{c}^{2}}\\right)[{({\\bar{v}}_{i}-\\bar{v})}^{2}+{({\\sigma }_{i}-{\\sigma }_{c})}^{2}],\\end{eqnarray}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\t2\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>where <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/834/2/210/Full/apjaa3c3eieqn62.gif\" alt=\"${\\bar{v}}_{i}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{\\bar{v}}_{i}</script></span></span> and <em>\u03c3</em><sub><em>i</em></sub> correspond to the mean and standard deviation of the velocity distribution of the <em>N</em><sub>local</sub> members closest to the <em>i</em>th member (included), while <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/834/2/210/Full/apjaa3c3eieqn63.gif\" alt=\"$\\bar{v}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">\\bar{v}</script></span></span> and <em>\u03c3</em><sub><em>c</em></sub> correspond to the mean velocity and velocity dispersion using all available cluster members, respectively. The null hypothesis of the DS test has no correlation between position and velocity, i.e., the mean velocity and dispersion should be the same locally as globally (within counting statistic). The <em>P</em>-values for the DS test are calculated as in Hou et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib52\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib52\">2012</a>); by comparing the observed substructure estimator \u0394<sub>obs</sub> to the shuffled values \u0394<sub>shuffled</sub>, which are computed by randomly shuffling the observed velocities and reassigning these values to the member positions via 1000 Monte Carlo (MC) simulations, and by taking <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/834/2/210/Full/apjaa3c3eieqn64.gif\" alt=\"${N}_{{\\rm{local}}}=\\sqrt{{N}_{{\\rm{mem}}}}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{N}_{{\\rm{local}}}=\\sqrt{{N}_{{\\rm{mem}}}}</script></span></span>. The <em>P</em>-values are given by</p><div xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"display-eqn\" id=\"apjaa3c3eeqn3\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/834/2/210/Full/apjaa3c3eeqn3.gif\" alt=\"Equation (3)\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex; mode=display\">\\begin{eqnarray}&&P=\\sum ({{\\rm{\\Delta }}}_{{\\rm{shuffled}}}-{{\\rm{\\Delta }}}_{{\\rm{obs}}})/{n}_{{\\rm{shuffle}}},\\end{eqnarray}\n\t\t\t\t\\tag{\n\t\t\t\t3\n\t\t\t\t}\n\t\t\t</script></span></div><p>where <em>n</em><sub>shuffle</sub> is the number of MC simulations used to compute the probability. The statistic of the DS test is performed by 100 realizations of the process above, with the central value given by the mean of the <em>P</em>-value\u00a0distribution and the uncertainties given by the upper and lower 68% confidence intervals. One can see from Equation (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"eqnref\" href=\"#apjaa3c3eeqn3\">3</a>) that clusters with significant substructure will have low <em>P</em>-values, since it is unlikely to obtain \u0394<sub>obs</sub> randomly. We have therefore set the threshold for substructure detection to a significance level (s.l.) of 0.05 within uncertainties, where false detections are not expected given the size of our sample (Pinkney et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib65\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib65\">1996</a>; Sif\u00f3n et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib72\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib72\">2013</a>). The results are listed in Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjaa3c3et4\">4</a>. There are 10 out of 24 clusters (<em>N</em><sub>mem</sub> <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/gsim.gif\" alt=\"gsim\" align=\"baseline\" /> 20) that have rejected the null hypothesis, indicating signs of dynamical substructure, which is consistent with previous optical and X-ray studies of local clusters (Girardi et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib40\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib40\">1997</a>; Schuecker et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib70\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib70\">2001</a>). However, high values of \u0394 might also be obtained when the velocities are shuffled, leading to a higher probability-to-exceed or a lower significance detection of substructure, resulting in erroneous assumptions that are consistent with no substructure (White et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib82\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib82\">2010</a>). Furthermore, the DS test (and almost all substructure indicators) is highly viewing-angle dependent,\u00a0which complicates the inferences about the dynamical state of the clusters.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjaa3c3ef10\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjaa3c3ef10\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/834/2/210/Full/apjaa3c3ef10_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 10.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 10.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/834/2/210/Full/apjaa3c3ef10_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 10.</strong>\u00a0From the top-left to bottom-right panels, we show the SL-selected galaxy clusters RCS2 J1526+0432, SDSS J2111\u22120114, SDSS J2135\u22120102, and RCS2 J2147\u22120102.
