As in previous panels, the image covers a field of view of 75''\u00a0<b>&#x00d7;</b>\u00a075''.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjaa3c3ef12\" href=\"/0004-637X/834/2/210/downloadFigure/figure/apjaa3c3ef12\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjaa3c3ef12\" href=\"/0004-637X/834/2/210/downloadHRFigure/figure/apjaa3c3ef12\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjaa3c3ef13\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjaa3c3ef13\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/834/2/210/Full/apjaa3c3ef13_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 13.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 13.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/834/2/210/Full/apjaa3c3ef13_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 13.</strong>\u00a0Redshift distribution of our cluster sample, which spans a wide redshift range 0.22\u00a0\u2264\u00a0<em>z</em>\u00a0\u2264\u00a01.01.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjaa3c3ef13\" href=\"/0004-637X/834/2/210/downloadFigure/figure/apjaa3c3ef13\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjaa3c3ef13\" href=\"/0004-637X/834/2/210/downloadHRFigure/figure/apjaa3c3ef13\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><p>Since substructure does not affect the velocity dispersion measurements when the clusters under consideration have a uni-modal velocity distribution (Girardi et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib40\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib40\">1997</a>; Evrard et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib33\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib33\">2008</a>; Sif\u00f3n et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib72\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib72\">2013</a>), we apply the Kolmogorov-Smirnov test (KS; Hou et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib51\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib51\">2009</a>, and references therein) to those clusters in our sample with <em>N</em><sub>mem</sub> <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/gsim.gif\" alt=\"gsim\" align=\"baseline\" /> 20, to corroborate whether their velocity distributions are consistent with a Gaussian distribution. The KS test is a non-parametric hypothesis test based on the <em>supremum</em> statistics that measures the distance between the empirical distribution function (EDF) of a sample and the cumulative distribution function (CDF) of a chosen reference distribution. We set the mean and variance of the reference distribution to the mean velocity and velocity dispersion squared of each cluster, respectively. The statistic of the KS test is calculated by 5000 bootstrapped realizations of the velocity data of each cluster, with the final probability given by the mean of the <em>P</em>-values distribution and the uncertainties given by the upper and lower 68% confidence intervals. The threshold for rejecting the null hypothesis, that the sample is drawn from the reference distribution, is set to an s.l. of 0.05 within uncertainties. The velocity distributions of 23 out of 24 clusters (with <em>N</em><sub>mem</sub> <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/gsim.gif\" alt=\"gsim\" align=\"baseline\" /> 20) show high <em>P</em>-values, suggesting that the deviation between the velocity data of these clusters and a Gaussian distribution is smaller than one would expect to arise from two different distributions. We therefore assume that the velocity distributions of these 23 SL clusters are uni-modal; consequently, we can conclude that their velocity dispersion measurements are robust and that their mass estimates are representative of their total masses, i.e., Equation (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"eqnref\" href=\"#apjaa3c3eeqn1\">1</a>) is valid for this subset. The results are listed in Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjaa3c3et4\">4</a>. For completeness, we also apply a Gaussian fit to the binned data of the velocity histograms of these clusters. In all cases, the best-fitting Gaussian parameters are consistent with the mean velocity and velocity dispersion squared within 1<em>\u03c3</em>, though the fit usually depends on the choice of the bin width. Their velocity histograms together with their respective Gaussian fits are shown in Figures <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ef14\">14</a> and <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ef15\">15</a>.</p><p>RCS2 J0309\u22121437 is the only system in our sample (with <em>N</em><sub>mem</sub> <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/gsim.gif\" alt=\"gsim\" align=\"baseline\" /> 20) where the null hypothesis of the KS test is rejected, suggesting that its velocity data is not consistent with a uni-modal distribution. Nonetheless, we list its velocity dispersion and virial mass in Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjaa3c3et4\">4</a>, along with the estimates for five systems with relatively few spectroscopically confirmed cluster members (<span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/834/2/210/Full/apjaa3c3eieqn65.gif\" alt=\"$\\langle {N}_{{\\rm{mem}}}\\rangle \\sim 10$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">\\langle {N}_{{\\rm{mem}}}\\rangle \\sim 10</script></span></span>). It should be noted that the measurements for these six systems represent only a first guess and should not be considered as the final estimates. The velocity histograms for these clusters are shown in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ef16\">16</a>, in Appendix A.</p></div><h3 id=\"apjaa3c3es5-2\" name=\"apjaa3c3es5-2\">5.2.\u00a0Region of Cluster Members</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>Observational and theoretical studies have shown that velocity dispersion profiles are flat from ~0.6\u20130.8 <em>r</em><sub>200</sub> outward (Fadda et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib35\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib35\">1996</a>; Biviano &amp; Girardi <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib13\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib13\">2003</a>; Faltenbacher &amp; Diemand <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib36\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib36\">2006</a>)\u00a0and highly biased in the innermost regions of the clusters (~0.2 <em>r</em><sub>200</sub>). Hence, estimating the effect of this potential sampling bias requires the knowledge of the virial radius for each cluster and also its projected angular size on the sky. We derive the virial radius of all clusters in our sample by assuming spherical symmetry<sup xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"fnref\"><a class=\"fnref\" href=\"#apjaa3c3efn30\">20</a></sup>\n and using the previous results of <em>M</em><sub>200</sub> (i.e., <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/834/2/210/Full/apjaa3c3eieqn66.gif\" alt=\"${M}_{200}=200{\\rho }_{c}\\times 4\\pi {r}_{200}^{3}/3$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{M}_{200}=200{\\rho }_{c}\\times 4\\pi {r}_{200}^{3}/3</script></span></span>), resulting in a median virial radius of <em>r</em><sub>200</sub>\u00a0=\u00a00.88 Mpc <em>h</em><sup>\u22121</sup> that corresponds to an angular size on the sky of <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/834/2/210/Full/apjaa3c3eieqn67.gif\" alt=\"${\\theta }_{{r}_{200}}=3\\buildrel{\\prime\\prime}\\over{.} 5$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{\\theta }_{{r}_{200}}=3\\buildrel{\\prime\\prime}\\over{.} 5</script></span></span>. Since our field of view has an average angular radius of <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/834/2/210/Full/apjaa3c3eieqn68.gif\" alt=\"$\\sim 3\\buildrel{\\prime\\prime}\\over{.} 2$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">\\sim 3\\buildrel{\\prime\\prime}\\over{.} 2</script></span></span> from the cluster centers, we are sampling cluster galaxies until approximately 0.9 <em>r</em><sub>200</sub> of the clusters. Therefore, the field restriction should not bias our results because the flatness of the velocity dispersion profiles begins to smaller radii. Furthermore, since we are mainly sampling cluster members on average within ~0.9 <em>r</em><sub>200</sub> (Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ef17\">17</a>), the probability to include interlopers is approximately 30% lower than for sampling until\u00a0twice <em>r</em><sub>200</sub> (White et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib82\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib82\">2010</a>; Saro et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib69\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib69\">2013</a>).</p></div><h3 id=\"apjaa3c3es5-3\" name=\"apjaa3c3es5-3\">5.3.\u00a0Number of Cluster Members</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>The number of cluster members plays a decisive role in the analysis of dynamical cluster properties and velocity dispersion measurements (Biviano et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib14\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib14\">2006</a>; Wojtak &amp; \u0141okas <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib85\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib85\">2010</a>). White et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib82\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib82\">2010</a>), using high-resolution <em>N</em>-body simulations, studied the stability of the cluster velocity dispersion as a function of the number of subhalos used to estimate it. They found that the results are generally stable once <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/gsim.gif\" alt=\"gsim\" align=\"baseline\" />50 subhalos are included; however, there is an intrinsic scatter of ~10% between the line-of-sight velocity dispersion and the DM halo velocity dispersion, mainly due to the halo triaxiality. They also found that the line-of-sight velocity dispersion is biased low when a small number of subhalos is used (increasing the scatter), but estimates with <em>N</em><sub>mem</sub> <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/gsim.gif\" alt=\"gsim\" align=\"baseline\" /> 20 tend asymptotically to the true velocity dispersion of clusters. Indeed, there is a general consensus in the literature that identifying on the order of 20 member galaxies is sufficient to derive the velocity dispersion of clusters (Beers et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib12\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib12\">1990</a>; Aguerri &amp; S\u00e1nchez-Janssen <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib2\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib2\">2010</a>; Hou et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib52\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib52\">2012</a>), though there could be an ~15% underestimation if only brighter galaxies are used, indicating that dynamical friction of brighter galaxies has a significant impact on the measured velocity dispersion (Old et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib64\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib64\">2013</a>; Saro et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib69\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib69\">2013</a>).</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjaa3c3ef14\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjaa3c3ef14\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/834/2/210/Full/apjaa3c3ef14_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 14.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 14.</strong>\u00a0Velocity histograms for 12 out of 23 of our clusters with <em>N</em><sub>mem</sub> <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/gsim.gif\" alt=\"gsim\" align=\"baseline\" /> 20 showing a uni-modal distribution. Also, note that the <em>y</em>-axes differ between plots.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjaa3c3ef14\" href=\"/0004-637X/834/2/210/downloadFigure/figure/apjaa3c3ef14\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjaa3c3ef14\" href=\"/0004-637X/834/2/210/downloadHRFigure/figure/apjaa3c3ef14\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjaa3c3ef15\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjaa3c3ef15\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/834/2/210/Full/apjaa3c3ef15_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 15.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 17.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/834/2/210/Full/apjaa3c3ef17_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 17.</strong>\u00a0Stacked analysis of our 29 galaxy clusters. The peculiar velocity of each galaxy normalized by the velocity dispersion <em>\u03c3</em><sub><em>c</em></sub> of the corresponding cluster vs. the cluster-centric distance normalized by <em>r</em><sub>200</sub> for each cluster is shown.</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjaa3c3ef17\" href=\"/0004-637X/834/2/210/downloadFigure/figure/apjaa3c3ef17\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjaa3c3ef17\" href=\"/0004-637X/834/2/210/downloadHRFigure/figure/apjaa3c3ef17\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><p>As mentioned in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaa3c3es3\">3</a>, we have recovered a total of 1004 spectroscopically confirmed cluster members, which gives us an average of <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/834/2/210/Full/apjaa3c3eieqn69.gif\" alt=\"$\\langle {N}_{{\\rm{mem}}}\\rangle \\sim 35$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">\\langle {N}_{{\\rm{mem}}}\\rangle \\sim 35</script></span></span> member galaxies per cluster. Therefore, we could assume that our estimates represent the \"true\" line-of-sight velocity dispersion of our clusters based on the studies above. However, we need to explore if these measurements are affected by dynamical friction since our spectroscopic strategy was to prioritize brighter galaxies ahead of fainter ones. In order to probe this possible systematic bias, we analyze the behavior of the velocity dispersion as a function of the number of brighter cluster members of six\u00a0clusters from our sample with <em>N</em><sub>mem</sub> <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/gsim.gif\" alt=\"gsim\" align=\"baseline\" /> 50. We rank the member galaxies according to their absolute <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/834/2/210/Full/apjaa3c3eieqn70.gif\" alt=\"$r^{\\prime} -$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">r^{\\prime} -</script></span></span> band magnitude and calculate the line-of-sight velocity dispersion using the brightest galaxies from <em>N</em><sub>mem</sub>\u00a0=\u00a08 to <em>N</em><sub>mem</sub>\u00a0=\u00a0<em>N</em><sub>total</sub>. We plot the resultant velocity dispersions <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/834/2/210/Full/apjaa3c3eieqn71.gif\" alt=\"$\\sigma ({N}_{{\\rm{mem}}})$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">\\sigma ({N}_{{\\rm{mem}}})</script></span></span> in Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ef18\">18</a> (top panel), normalized by the final dispersion <em>\u03c3</em><sub><em>c</em></sub>, obtained using all available cluster members. As showed by previous studies, the scatter between <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/834/2/210/Full/apjaa3c3eieqn72.gif\" alt=\"$\\sigma ({N}_{{\\rm{mem}}})$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">\\sigma ({N}_{{\\rm{mem}}})</script></span></span> and <em>\u03c3</em><sub><em>c</em></sub> increases considerably for a lower number of cluster members (<em>N</em><sub>mem</sub>\u00a0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/lesssim.gif\" alt=\"lesssim\" align=\"baseline\" />\u00a015), showing a clear underestimation of <em>\u03c3</em> when <em>N</em><sub>mem</sub>\u00a0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/lesssim.gif\" alt=\"lesssim\" align=\"baseline\" />\u00a010. For measurements using <em>N</em><sub>mem</sub> <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/gsim.gif\" alt=\"gsim\" align=\"baseline\" /> 20, the scatter is almost symmetric, i.e., the bias introduced by using only a fraction of the total number of member galaxies may underestimate or overestimate the \"true\" line-of-sight velocity dispersions, and whether the bias is from above or below depends upon the cluster under consideration (e.g., White et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib82\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib82\">2010</a>). Therefore, estimates using <em>N</em><sub>mem</sub> <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/gsim.gif\" alt=\"gsim\" align=\"baseline\" /> 20 are only slightly affected by dynamical friction; pointing out that the most likely effect that is affecting our measurements should be associated to the lack of galaxy tracers.</p><figure xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" id=\"apjaa3c3ef18\" tabindex=\"-1\" role=\"group\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\" data-toolbar-type=\"figure\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjaa3c3ef18\" data-toolbar-img=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/834/2/210/Full/apjaa3c3ef18_lr.jpg\" data-toolbar-title=\"Figure 18.\"><figure><div class=\"panzoom-container\"><div class=\"panzoom-parent\" style=\"overflow: hidden; position: relative;\"><img class=\"panzoom\" alt=\"Figure 18.\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/834/2/210/Full/apjaa3c3ef18_lr.jpg\" style=\"transform: matrix(1, 0, 0, 1, 0, 0); backface-visibility: hidden; transform-origin: 50% 50% 0px; cursor: move; transition: transform 200ms ease-in-out 0s;\"></img></div><div class=\"buttons zoom-tools\"><button class=\"zoom-in\"><span class=\"icon-zoomin\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom In\n\t\t\t\t</button><button class=\"zoom-out\"><span class=\"icon-zoomout\"></span>\n\t                 Zoom Out\n\t\t\t\t</button><span class=\"mobile-block\"><button class=\"reset\"><span class=\"icon-loop\"></span>\n\t                     Reset image size\n\t\t\t\t\t</button></span></div></div><figcaption><div class=\"article-text figure-caption\"><p><strong>Figure 18.</strong>\u00a0Top panel shows the line-of-sight velocity dispersion vs. the number of cluster members ordered by luminosity (from highest to lowest) for six\u00a0clusters from our SL lensing sample with more than 49 member galaxies. The bottom panel shows the line-of-sight velocity dispersion but with the inclusion of randomly selected <em>N</em><sub>mem</sub> member galaxies. In both panels, the resultant line-of-sight velocity dispersion has been normalized by the total dispersion of the corresponding cluster, <em>\u03c3</em><sub><em>c</em></sub>. In both cases, the scatter is symmetric about the ration of 1 when <em>N</em><sub>mem</sub> <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/gsim.gif\" alt=\"gsim\" align=\"baseline\" /> 20, indicating that the estimates are mainly affected by the lack of galaxy tracers rather than by dynamical friction. The cluster RCS2 J0047+0507 is represented by \"+\"; RCS2 J1517+1003 by \"<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/lowast.gif\" alt=\"lowast\" align=\"baseline\" />\"; RCS2 J1519+0840 by \"\u25e6\"; SDSS J2135\u22120102 by \"<span class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/834/2/210/Full/apjaa3c3eieqn73.gif\" alt=\"$\\diamond $\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">\\diamond</script></span></span>\"; RCS2 J2151\u22120138 by \"<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/bigtriangleup.gif\" alt=\"bigtriangleup\" align=\"baseline\" />\"; and the cluster RCS2 J2329\u22121317 by \"<span class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/834/2/210/Full/apjaa3c3eieqn74.gif\" alt=\"$\\square $\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">\\square</script></span></span>.\"</p></div><p class=\"mb-05 print-hide\">Download figure:</p><span class=\"btn-multi-block print-hide\"><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-std-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-std-img-apjaa3c3ef18\" href=\"/0004-637X/834/2/210/downloadFigure/figure/apjaa3c3ef18\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> Standard image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a><a class=\"btn btn-primary fig-dwnld-hi-img\" id=\"wd-jnl-art-btn-hires-img-apjaa3c3ef18\" href=\"/0004-637X/834/2/210/downloadHRFigure/figure/apjaa3c3ef18\"><span class=\"icon-image\"></span> High-resolution image\n\t\t\t\t\t</a></span></figcaption></figure></figure><p>To corroborate these conclusions, we repeat the previous process, but we\u00a0choose\u00a0the member galaxies randomly with respect to their absolute <em>r</em>-band magnitude. The results are shown in the bottom panel of Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ef18\">18</a>. In this figure,\u00a0one can easily see that for estimates using <em>N</em><sub>mem</sub> <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/gsim.gif\" alt=\"gsim\" align=\"baseline\" /> 20, the scatter around the \"true\" line-of-sight velocity dispersion is symmetric and similar to the scatter obtained when cluster members are sorted by their brightness. Therefore, we can assume that velocity dispersion estimates are mainly affected by the lack of cluster members rather than by dynamical friction. Indeed, in both cases of Figure <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ef18\">18</a>, the average scatter is of the order of ~10% when <em>N</em><sub>mem</sub> is equal to the average number of cluster members, i.e., <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/834/2/210/Full/apjaa3c3eieqn75.gif\" alt=\"${N}_{{\\rm{mem}}}=\\langle {N}_{{\\rm{mem}}}\\rangle =35$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{N}_{{\\rm{mem}}}=\\langle {N}_{{\\rm{mem}}}\\rangle =35</script></span></span>. It is to be noted that this 10% of scatter should be considered as the lower scatter that we should include in our velocity dispersion estimates in order to take into account the lack of cluster members. However, we leave this discussion for future observational and simulation studies, where we could compare results from a larger number of cluster members with specific simulations for the SL galaxy cluster population.</p></div><h3 id=\"apjaa3c3es5-4\" name=\"apjaa3c3es5-4\">5.4.\u00a0Line-of-sight Effect</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>It is well known that virialized halos that host galaxy clusters are triaxial (Thomas &amp; Couchman <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib74\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib74\">1992</a>; Warren et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib81\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib81\">1992</a>; Jing &amp; Suto <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib53\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib53\">2002</a>), with the major axis approximately twice as long as the minor axes,\u00a0which are approximately equal in size. This prolate shape could potentially lead to a bias in the velocity dispersion estimates because the velocity tensor is quite anisotropic and generally well aligned with the inertia tensor, with a typical misalignment angle of ~30 deg (Tormen et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib75\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib75\">1997</a>; Kasun &amp; Evrard <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib54\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib54\">2005</a>; White et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib82\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib82\">2010</a>). In other words, the\u00a0viewing along the major axis may contribute to a higher velocity dispersion, while the two minor axes to lower values; hence, the final measurements depend significantly on the chosen line of sight. In fact, numerical simulations have shown that, although the 3D velocity dispersion of DM particles within <em>r</em><sub>200</sub> is well correlated with <em>M</em><sub>200</sub> and the galaxies show little velocity bias compared to the DM particles, the line-of-sight velocity dispersions show a considerably larger scatter and the mass estimates from these measurements are biased with respect to the true values (Evrard et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib33\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib33\">2008</a>; White et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib82\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib82\">2010</a>; Saro et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib69\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib69\">2013</a>).</p><p>Studies based on simulations of SL halos have shown that the most effective strong lenses are not more triaxial than the general halo population (Hennawi et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib45\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib45\">2007</a>; Meneghetti et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib60\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib60\">2010</a>); however, they are more likely to have their major axes aligned along the line of sight. Therefore, we should assume that the velocity dispersion estimates for a sample of SL-selected clusters will be biased high with respect to the velocity dispersions measured for clusters that are randomly oriented on the sky. However, the intrinsic misalignment between the halo positional ellipsoid and the velocity tensor introduces an element of randomization into the orientation of the velocity tensor with respect to the line of sight that reduces the impact of the orientation bias. Specific predictions for the magnitude of this bias require the convolution of the probability distributions for the position orientation angle of the SL-selected clusters with their velocity principal axes. Since we do not have these probability distributions, we leave this additional correction for future analyses and, as in the previous section, we do not include this extra uncertainty in our estimates. However, it is important to note that our line-of-sight velocity dispersion measurements should have, at least, a scatter of the same order as found by White et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib82\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib82\">2010</a>) for the normal cluster population, i.e., a scatter of the order of ~10% between the line of sight and 3D velocity dispersions.</p></div></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjaa3c3es6\" name=\"apjaa3c3es6\">6.\u00a0SUMMARY AND CONCLUSIONS</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>We have conducted a large spectroscopic follow-up program of 29 SL-selected galaxy clusters discovered in the RCS-2 survey. Our spectroscopic analysis has revealed the nature of 52 gravitational arcs present in the core of our galaxy clusters, which correspond to 35 background sources at high\u00a0redshifts that are clearly distorted by the gravitational potential of these clusters. These lensed galaxies span a wide redshift range of 0.8\u00a0\u2264\u00a0<em>z</em>\u00a0\u2264\u00a02.9, with a median redshift of <em>z</em><sub><em>s</em></sub>\u00a0=\u00a01.8\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.1, that matches the expectations. This data\u00a0set extends the number of galaxy clusters with spectroscopic confirmation of their SL features that are available to perform lensing reconstructions of their mass distribution, especially at <em>z</em> <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/gsim.gif\" alt=\"gsim\" align=\"baseline\" /> 0.2.</p><p>This campaign has also yielded a total of 1004 spectroscopically confirmed cluster members that gives an average of <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/834/2/210/Full/apjaa3c3eieqn76.gif\" alt=\"$\\langle {N}_{{\\rm{mem}}}\\rangle \\sim 35$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">\\langle {N}_{{\\rm{mem}}}\\rangle \\sim 35</script></span></span> member galaxies per cluster. These data allow us to obtain robust redshifts for each cluster and measure velocity dispersions with a relatively high confidence level, which are translated into dynamical masses by using the Evrard et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib33\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib33\">2008</a>) <em>\u03c3</em><sub>DM</sub>\u00a0\u2212\u00a0<em>M</em><sub>200</sub> scaling relation. The redshift and mass ranges of our SL sample are distributed from 0.22\u00a0\u2264\u00a0<em>z</em>\u00a0\u2264\u00a01.01 and <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/834/2/210/Full/apjaa3c3eieqn77.gif\" alt=\"$5\\times {10}^{13}\\leqslant {M}_{200}/{h}_{70}^{-1}\\,{M}_{\\odot }\\leqslant 1.9\\times {10}^{15}$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">5\\times {10}^{13}\\leqslant {M}_{200}/{h}_{70}^{-1}\\,{M}_{\\odot }\\leqslant 1.9\\times {10}^{15}</script></span></span>, respectively. These wide redshift and mass ranges allow diverse kinds of studies: from the analysis of the relationship between the concentration of the cluster halos and their masses to studies of galaxy evolution in cluster environments.</p><p>We have analyzed some effects that could affect our velocity dispersion measurements, such as the presence of substructure in our clusters, the region where cluster members were selected, the final number of confirmed members, and the line-of-sight effects. Our primary conclusions are as follows.</p><ul class=\"clear-list wd-inline-list\"><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"\" id=\"\">1.\u00a0\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\">We found that 10 out of 24 of our clusters, where analysis is possible, show signs of dynamical substructure, which is consistent with previous optical and X-ray studies of local clusters. The velocity distributions of 23 of these clusters are consistent with a uni-modal distribution and we therefore assumed that Equation (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"eqnref\" href=\"#apjaa3c3eeqn1\">1</a>) is applicable to these SL clusters in our sample.</div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"\" id=\"\">2.\u00a0\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\">We sampled cluster member galaxies within the inner ~0.9 <em>r</em><sub>200</sub> of the clusters, which did not bias our results due to the flatness of the velocity dispersion profiles from ~0.8 <em>r</em><sub>200</sub>. Furthermore, since we mainly sampled cluster members within the virial radius, the probability to include interlopers is approximately 30% lower than for sampling up to twice <em>r</em><sub>200</sub> (Old et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib64\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib64\">2013</a>; Saro et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib69\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib69\">2013</a>).</div></li><li class=\"indices-list\"><div class=\"indices-id\" name=\"\" id=\"\">3.\u00a0\u00a0</div><div class=\"indices-content\">We have found that using <em>N</em><sub>mem</sub> <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/gsim.gif\" alt=\"gsim\" align=\"baseline\" /> 20, our velocity dispersion estimates are mainly affected by the lack of galaxy tracers rather than by dynamical friction. We found that, in both cases, sorting the cluster members by their brightness or randomly, the scatter is symmetric and of the order of ~10% when <span xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"inline-eqn\"><span class=\"tex\"><span class=\"texImage\"><img src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/834/2/210/Full/apjaa3c3eieqn78.gif\" alt=\"${N}_{{\\rm{mem}}}=\\langle {N}_{{\\rm{mem}}}\\rangle =35$\" align=\"top\"></img></span><script type=\"math/tex\">{N}_{{\\rm{mem}}}=\\langle {N}_{{\\rm{mem}}}\\rangle =35</script></span></span>. However, it is only a first guess of the magnitude of this potential bias. A better understanding of this effect needs deeper studies of the galaxy kinematics in SL clusters.</div></li></ul><p>\n</p><p>Summing up, we have found that our velocity dispersion measurements should be affected by the lack of cluster members as well as by the scatter between the line of sight and 3D velocity dispersion. However, specific predictions for the magnitude of these biases require an improvement in both simulation and observational studies, with larger simulations oriented to the SL galaxy cluster population and more spectroscopic information of the cluster members. Even though our measurements may be biased or may have large uncertainties, which are translated into large errors in the mass estimates, they serve as a base for a better understanding of the SL cluster properties. Furthermore, these dynamical masses can be used as <em>priors</em> for mass reconstruction studies, that combined with SL signatures yield one of the most robust measurements of the mass distribution of SL clusters.</p><p>A complete characterization of the properties and biases of this population is crucial for taking full advantage of future SL samples coming from a new era of large area deep imaging surveys (e.g., PanSTARRS, LSST, DES). The data and analysis presented in this work represent the first steps in this direction and also pave the way for multi-wavelength studies, i.e., when SL information is combined with WL, dynamical masses, X-ray, and SZe data. These kinds of studies will allow us to quantify the biases between the different observable masses in order to fully exploit the additional information provided by SL signatures, which can then be intelligently applied to scaling relations and mass estimates for the general cluster population.</p></div> <div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>M.C. gives particular thanks to Matthias Bartelmann, Matteo Maturi, Agnese Fabris, Jorge Gonz\u00e1lez, and Cristobal Sif\u00f3n for useful discussions and collaborations in this work. M.C.'s research is supported by the Transregional Collaborative Research Centre TRR 33. M.C. acknowledges the support of ESO through the ESO Studentship and DAA through the PUC-HD Graduate Exchange Fellowship. We thank to ESO and its support staff for the implementation of the Programmes 081.A-0561, 083.A-0491, and 084.A-0317. Support for L.F.B.\u00a0and T.A. is provided by the Ministry of Economy, Development, and Tourism's Millennium Science Initiative through grant IC120009, awarded to The Millennium Institute of Astrophysics, MAS. L.F.B.'s research is supported by proyecto FONDECYT 1120676. T.A.\u00a0acknowledges support from\u00a0proyecto FONDECYT 11130630. D.G.G. acknowledges financial support from the National Research Foundation (NRF) of South Africa.</p></div></div><!-- End article full text -->\n<!-- Start Footnotes -->\n\t<h2 <div class=\"overlay-text\">\n            <p>The American Astronomical Society (AAS), established in 1899 and based in Washington, DC, is the major organization of professional astronomers in North America. Its membership of about 7,000 individuals also includes physicists, mathematicians, geologists, engineers, and others whose research and educational interests lie within the broad spectrum of subjects comprising contemporary astronomy. The mission of the AAS is to enhance and share humanity's scientific understanding of the universe.</p> <p><a href=\"https://aas.org/\">https://aas.org/</a></div>\n        <p class=\"ta-c mt-1 mb-2\"><a href=\"\"></a></p>\n    </div>\n</span>\n<!-- Partner logo tile ends -->\n\n<!-- Partner logo tile starts -->\n<a href=\"\" class=\"overlay-launch\">\n    <img border=\"0\" src=\"https://cms.iopscience.org/7ebd1b32-0439-11e9-b401-cfe9679c40e3/iop-2016.png?guest=true\" alt=\"The Institute of Physics, find out more\">\n</a>\n<span class=\"overlay-set\">\n    <div class=\"tint-screen\"></div>\n    <div class=\"overlay-panel\">\n        <a title=\"Close\"  class=\"close-icon close-overlay\" tabindex=\"0\" role=\"button\" aria-describedby=\"close-icon-description\"><span class=\"icon-close\"></span></a>\n        <p id=\"close-icon-description\" class=\"offscreen-hidden\">Click here to close this overlay, or press the \"Escape\" key on your keyboard.</p>\n        <div class=\"overlay-img\">\n            <a href=\"\">\n                <img border=\"0\" src=\"https://cms.iopscience.org/7ebd1b32-0439-11e9-b401-cfe9679c40e3/iop-2016.png?guest=true\" alt=\"The Institute of Physics, find out more\">\n            </a>\n        </div>\n        <div class=\"overlay-text\">\n            <p>The Institute of Physics (IOP) is a leading scientific society promoting physics and bringing physicists together for the benefit of all. It has a worldwide membership of around 50 000 comprising physicists from all sectors, as well as those with an interest in physics. It works to advance physics research, application and education; and engages with policy makers and the public to develop awareness and understanding of physics. Its publishing company, IOP Publishing, is a world leader in professional scientific communications.</p> <p><a href=\"https://www.iop.org\">https://www.iop.org</a></p></div>\n        <p class=\"ta-c mt-1 mb-2\"><a href=\"\"></a></p>\n    </div>\n</span>\n<!-- Partner logo tile ends -->\n\n<p></p>\n                </div>\n        </div>\n    </div>\n<!-- Branded journal header ends -->\n</div> <!-- end secondary-header -->\n<!-- Secondary header ends --><div class=\"da1-da2\" itemscope=\"\" itemtype=\"http://schema.org/ScholarlyArticle\" id=\"page-content\">\n            <div class=\"da1 ta1 article-head\">\n                <!-- Start Eyebrow block, containing Surtitle and Labels -->\n<div class=\"eyebrow\">\n\t<!-- Start Collection Labels -->\n    <!-- End Collection Labels --> </div>\n<!-- End Eyebrow block -->\n\n<h1 itemprop=\"headline\" class=\"wd-jnl-art-title\">VLT/<I>MAGELLAN</I> SPECTROSCOPY OF 29 STRONG LENSING SELECTED GALAXY CLUSTERS</h1>\n\n<p class=\"mb-0\">\n\t<span data-authors=\"\">\n\t\t<span itemtype=\"http://schema.org/Person\" itemprop=\"author\" class=\"nowrap\"><span itemprop=\"name\">Mauricio Carrasco</span><sup>1,2</sup></span>, <span itemtype=\"http://schema.org/Person\" itemprop=\"author\" class=\"nowrap\"><span itemprop=\"name\">L. <span class=\"icon-newtab\">\n            </a></p>\n        </div>\n\n    <!-- End Email Alert -->\n<!-- End Email Alert --><!-- Start Citation Alert Link -->\n                        <p>\n<div class=\"jnl-notifications-wrapper\">\n    <a class=\"wd-jnl-email-alert\" href=\"https://myiopscience.iop.org/signin?origin=a0&return=https%3A%2F%2Fiopscience.iop.org%2Fmyiopscience%2Falerts%2Fsubscribe%3FarticleID%3D0004-637X%2F834%2F2%2F210\"><span\n            class=\"icon-alarm icon-alarm--jhp\"></span>Create citation alert</a>\n</div><!-- End Citation Alert Link -->\n                    </div>\n\t\t</div>\n    </div>\n    <!-- Article information ends -->\n<!-- Start Info top-->\n    <!-- end Info top --><div class=\"article-content\">\n\t<span id=\"articleId\" class=\"hide\">0004-637X/834/2/210</span>\n<!-- Start Abstract -->\n    <h2 id=\"artAbst\" class=\"collapse-blocked\">Abstract</h2>\n        <!-- Start article video abstract -->\n\t<!-- End article video abstract -->\n\t<div class=\"article-text wd-jnl-art-abstract cf\" itemprop=\"description\">\n            <P>We present an extensive spectroscopic follow-up campaign of 29 strong lensing (SL) selected galaxy clusters discovered primarily in the Second Red-Sequence Cluster Survey (RCS-2). Our spectroscopic analysis yields redshifts for 52 gravitational arcs present in the core of our galaxy clusters, which correspond to 35 distinct background sources that are clearly distorted by the gravitational potential of these clusters. These lensed galaxies span a wide redshift range of 0.8\u00a0\u2264\u00a0<I>z</I>\u00a0\u2264\u00a02.9, with a median redshift of <I>z</I><SUB><I>s</I></SUB>\u00a0=\u00a01.8\u00a0\u00b1\u00a00.1. We also measure reliable redshifts for 1004 cluster members, allowing us to obtain robust velocity dispersion measurements for 23 of these clusters, which we then use to determine their dynamical masses by using a simulation-based <I>\u03c3</I><SUB>DM</SUB>\u00a0\u2212\u00a0<I>M</I><SUB>200</SUB> scaling relation. The redshift and mass ranges covered by our SL sample are 0.22\u00a0\u2264\u00a0<I>z</I>\u00a0\u2264\u00a01.01 and <IMG ALIGN=\"MIDDLE\" alt=\"$5\\times {10}^{13}\\leqslant {M}_{200}/{h}_{70}^{-1}\\,{M}_{\\odot }\\leqslant 1.9\\times {10}^{15}$\" src=\"https://static.iopscience.com/2.44.0/img/lazy-loading-placeholder.gif\" data-src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/images/0004-637X/834/2/210/apjaa3c3eieqn1.gif\"></IMG>, respectively. We analyze and quantify some possible effects that might bias our mass estimates, such as the presence of substructure, the region where cluster members are selected for spectroscopic follow-up, the final number of confirmed members, and line-of-sight effects. We find that 10 clusters of our sample with <I>N</I><SUB>mem</SUB> <img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/gsim.gif\" alt=\"gsim\" align=\"baseline\" /> 20 show signs of dynamical substructure. However, the velocity data of only one system is inconsistent with a uni-modal distribution. We therefore assume that the substructures are only marginal and not of comparable size to the clusters themselves. Consequently, our velocity dispersion and mass estimates can be used as <I>priors</I> for SL mass reconstruction studies and also represent an important step toward a better understanding of the properties of the SL galaxy cluster population.</P></div>\n    <!-- End abstract -->\n<!-- Start Export citation and abstract tools -->\n\t<p>\n\t\t<small>Export citation and abstract</small>\n\t\t<span class=\"btn-multi-block\">\n\t\t\t<a title=\"Export BibTex\">BibTeX</a>\n\t\t\t<a href=\"/export?articleId=0004-637X/834/2/210&doi=10.3847/1538-4357/834/2/210&exportFormat=iopexport_ris&exportType=abs&navsubmit=Export+abstract\" class=\"btn btn-primary wd-btn-cit-abs-ris\" title=\"Export RIS\">RIS</a>\n\t\t</span>\n\t</p>\n<!-- End Export citation and abstract tools -->\n<!-- Start AAS Related Links -->\n<div href=\"/journal/0004-637X/page/About%20Related%20Links\" target=\"_blank\">About Related Links</a></li>\n      \t</ul>\n    </div>\n  </div>\n</div>\n<!-- Start info bottom -->\n    <!-- End info bottom -->\n<!-- Start article full text -->\n\t<div xmlns:book=\"http://api.iop.org/Book/1.0/\" itemprop=\"articleBody\" class=\"wd-jnl-art-full-text article-text\">\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjaa3c3es1\" name=\"apjaa3c3es1\">1.\u00a0INTRODUCTION</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><p>Galaxy clusters have long been considered one of the most important cosmological probes due to their privileged position in the hierarchical formation scenario as the most massive and virialized objects. As such, galaxy clusters can inform important tests of the standard concordance cosmological model, and provide unique laboratories for the understanding of dark matter (DM) properties (see Voit <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib80\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib80\">2005</a>, for a review). For example, the number density of galaxy clusters is sensitive to the amplitude of the primordial density fluctuations, and can therefore be used as a powerful tool for estimating cosmological parameters, so long as the masses and redshifts of clusters are measured with good precision (Evrard et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib34\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib34\">2002</a>; Vikhlinin et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib78\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib78\">2009</a>; Rozo et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib67\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib67\">2010</a>).</p><p>In recent years, extensive efforts have been made to determine the mass and redshift distribution of samples of galaxy clusters (B\u00f6hringer et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib16\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib16\">2004</a>; Gladders &amp; Yee <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib43\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib43\">2005</a>; Burenin et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib19\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib19\">2007</a>; Gilbank et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib39\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib39\">2011</a>; Bleem et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib15\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib15\">2015</a>). Most of these mass measurements are based on baryonic signatures\u2014e.g., optical light, X-ray emission, and the Sunyaev Zel'dovich effect (SZe). However, there are pernicious systematic uncertainties that affect these baryonic signatures, which trace only a small fraction of the total matter content of galaxy clusters. The most robust and direct way to map the total (baryonic and dark) matter distribution in galaxy clusters is through the analysis of gravitational lensing effects, whereby background galaxy profiles are modified by the gravitational potential of foreground clusters, producing shape distortions and apparent flux magnification (see reviews by Bartelmann <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib5\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib5\">2010</a>\u00a0and\u00a0Kneib &amp; Natarajan <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib56\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib56\">2011</a>).</p><p>In the outer regions of galaxy clusters, the gravitational potential produces only tiny distortions that do not constrain the cluster profile individually, and therefore it is necessary to perform statistical measurements of the distortions affecting large ensembles of background galaxies. This is the so-called weak lensing (WL) regime, and the application of WL measurements has become a powerful tool in the recent years (Dahle <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib25\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib25\">2006</a>; Hoekstra &amp; Jain <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib48\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib48\">2008</a>; Okabe et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib63\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib63\">2010</a>; High et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib47\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib47\">2012</a>; Umetsu et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib76\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib76\">2014</a>; van Uitert et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib77\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib77\">2015</a>). In the innermost region of clusters\u2014the strong lensing (SL) regime\u2014the mass density can be high enough to produce dramatic distortions in the form of giant elongated arcs or multiple images of background sources, which can inform high-precision mass reconstruction studies of the cluster core (e.g., Zitrin et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib92\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib92\">2009</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib93\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib93\">2012a</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib94\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib94\">2012b</a>; Limousin et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib59\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib59\">2012</a>). Statistical analysis of samples of galaxy clusters that exhibit the hallmarks\u2014giant arcs and multiply imaged sources\u2014of strong lensing can also be used in tests of the \u039bCDM cosmological model. For example, several different studies comparing observations and predictions for the abundance of giant arcs (\"giant arc statistics\") find that simulations under-predict the number of giant arcs on the sky by perhaps as much as an order of magnitude (Bartelmann et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib6\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib6\">1998</a>; Gladders et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib41\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib41\">2003</a>; Li et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib58\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib58\">2006</a>).</p><p>In addition to their cosmological applications, the large magnification effects that typically act on strongly lensed background galaxies allows for\u00a0the study of high-redshift galaxies, which would otherwise be too faint to be observed. However, cases of bright, high-magnification strong lensing remain relatively rare phenomena, occurring in only a\u00a0small fraction of galaxy clusters (Horesh et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib49\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib49\">2010</a>). It is therefore always valuable to identify and follow-up on new samples of strong lensing galaxy clusters. Identifying new strong lensing samples serves to effectively increase the volume of the early universe that is available for observations (e.g., Bradley et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib18\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib18\">2008</a>; Bouwens et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib17\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib17\">2009</a>; Zheng et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib90\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib90\">2009</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib91\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib91\">2012</a>; Wuyts et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib86\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib86\">2010</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib87\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib87\">2014</a>; Bayliss et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib10\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib10\">2011b</a>, <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib11\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib11\">2010</a>; Coe et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib23\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib23\">2013</a>).</p><p>In this paper, we present the results of a spectroscopic follow-up campaign of 29 SL-selected galaxy clusters with 6.5\u20138.2 m class telescopes, discovered primarily in the Second Red-Sequence Cluster Survey (RCS-2; Gilbank et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib39\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib39\">2011</a>). The data presented in this work reveal the nature of the giant arc and multiple-image candidates, increasing the number of spectroscopically confirmed lensed galaxies at high-redshift. From these data, we also identify redshifts for more than one thousand cluster member galaxies, which are used to estimate dynamical masses of these clusters. The combination of these results will allow us to derive robust reconstructions of the matter distribution in these galaxy clusters.</p><p>This paper is organized as follows. In Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaa3c3es2\">2,</a> we describe the cluster sample and summarize the spectroscopic follow-up campaign. In Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaa3c3es3\">3,</a> we present the redshift results for the lensed galaxies and cluster members, as well as the final redshift and velocity dispersion of the clusters. In Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaa3c3es4\">4,</a> we show the dynamical mass results, while, in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaa3c3es5\">5,</a>\u00a0we analyze the possible systematics that might affect our measurements. Lastly, we summarize the main results and present the final conclusions in Section <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"secref\" href=\"#apjaa3c3es6\">6</a>. Throughout the paper, we assume a flat \u039bCDM cosmology with \u03a9<sub><em>m</em></sub>\u00a0=\u00a00.27, \u03a9<sub>\u039b</sub>\u00a0=\u00a00.73, and <em>H</em><sub>0</sub>\u00a0=\u00a070 <em>h</em><sub>70</sub> km s<sup>\u22121</sup> Mpc<sup>\u22121</sup>.</p></div>\n<h2 class=\"header-anchor\" id=\"apjaa3c3es2\" name=\"apjaa3c3es2\">2.\u00a0OBSERVATIONS</h2><div class=\"article-text\" data-mobile-collapse=\"\"><h3 id=\"apjaa3c3es2-1\" name=\"apjaa3c3es2-1\">2.1.\u00a0The SL-selected Cluster Sample</h3><div class=\"article-text\"><p>The cluster sample presented here is a subset of a larger sample of more than one hundred SL-selected clusters that have been identified primarily in RCS-2 imaging data. The median seeing of the RCS-2 survey is ~0<img src=\"https://cdn.iopscience.com/icons/Entities/farcs.gif\" alt=\"farcs\" align=\"bottom\" />7, making it ideal for the detection and classification of giant arcs. Furthermore, the RCS-2 survey was designed to detect galaxy clusters out to <em>z</em>\u00a0~\u00a01.1 using the cluster red-sequence technique (Gladders &amp; Yee <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib42\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib42\">2000</a>) in deep wide-field images. We have searched the resulting galaxy cluster catalog for SL systems, where the SL selection criterion is the presence of one or more giant arcs around a galaxy cluster's core. The SL-selected clusters identified in the RCS-2 comprise a new sample of giant arcs, the Red-Sequence Cluster Survey Giant Arc (RCSGA; Bayliss <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib8\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib8\">2012</a>; M. D. Gladders et al. 2016, in preparation) catalog. Giant arc candidates are flagged by multiple members of our team performing independent visual inspections of the entire RCS-2 red-sequence catalog of galaxy clusters, and systems that are flagged by most or all inspectors constitute the SL cluster sample, as described in more detail by Bayliss (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib8\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib8\">2012</a>). We supplement the RCS-2 SL-selected cluster sample with a few systems chosen to fill R.A. gaps that were similarly selected from the Sloan Digital Sky Survey (SDSS; York et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib89\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib89\">2000</a>).</p><p>We performed a comprehensive spectroscopic follow-up of 29 SL-selected galaxy clusters primarily from the RCSGA; 7 of these clusters were previously unpublished. The cluster sample is presented in Table <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"tabref\" href=\"#apjaa3c3et1\">1</a>, where clusters formerly reported in other studies are marked with their corresponding references. We have adopted the naming convention described in Bayliss et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib10\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib10\">2011b</a>) for giant arcs discovered in RCSGA, given by RCSGA\u00a0\u2212\u00a0Jhhmmss+ddmmss (e.g., Wuyts et al. <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib86\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib86\">2010</a>; Bayliss <a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib8\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib8\">2012</a>).</p><div tabindex=\"-1\" id=\"apjaa3c3et1\" class=\"boxout boxout-bdr-grey keyboard-focus-only\"><p><b>Table 1.</b>\u00a0\nSummary of VLT/<em>Magellan</em> Spectroscopic Observations\n</p><table cellpadding=\"0\" cellspacing=\"0\" border=\"0\" data-toolbar-link=\"apjaa3c3et1\" data-toolbar-img=\"/0004-637X/834/2/210/suppdata/apjaa3c3et1_lr.gif\" data-toolbar-type=\"table\" data-toolbar-title=\"Table 1\"><colgroup><col align=\"left\" char=\"\"></col><col align=\"left\" char=\"\"></col><col align=\"left\" char=\"\"></col><col align=\"left\" char=\"\"></col><col align=\"left\" char=\"\"></col><col align=\"left\" char=\"\"></col></colgroup><thead>\n<tr valign=\"top\">\n<th scope=\"col\" colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"left\">\u00a0</th>\n<th scope=\"col\" colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">\u00a0</th>\n<th scope=\"col\" colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">\u00a0</th>\n<th scope=\"col\" colspan=\"2\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">Total Exposure Time</th>\n<th scope=\"col\" colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">\u00a0</th>\n</tr>\n<tr valign=\"top\">\n<th scope=\"col\" colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"left\">Target</th>\n<th scope=\"col\" colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">R.A.<sup>a</sup>\n</th>\n<th scope=\"col\" colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">Decl.<sup>a</sup>\n</th>\n<th scope=\"col\" colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">FORS2/VLT</th>\n<th scope=\"col\" colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">IMACS/<em>Magellan</em></th>\n<th scope=\"col\" colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">Prev. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib86\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib86\">2010</a>)</td>\n</tr>\n<tr valign=\"top\">\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"left\">RCS2 J0859\u22120345</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"char\" char=\".\">08 59 14.486</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"char\" char=\".\">\u221203 45 14.63</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"char\" char=\".\">3.33/2</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">...</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">Cabanac et al. (<a xmlns:xlink=\"http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink\" class=\"cite\" href=\"#apjaa3c3ebib79\" id=\"fnref-apjaa3c3ebib79\">1999</a>)</td>\n</tr>\n<tr valign=\"top\">\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"left\">SDSS J2313\u22120104</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"char\" char=\".\">23 13 54.514</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"char\" char=\".\">\u221201 04 48.46</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"char\" char=\".\">1.50/2</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">1.17/1</td>\n<td colspan=\"1\" rowspan=\"1\" align=\"center\">Geach et al.
